Expiation is Warded Off by Doubts
Legal maxim from Kitab al-Sawm on expiation and doubt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi maxim on expiation and apply it to scenarios of doubt in fasting.

Core Idea
Penal expiations are averted by uncertainty — doubt removes the obligation.

This lesson explores a fundamental principle in Hanafi fiqh: the maxim 'Expiation is warded off by doubts.' It serves as a leniency in rulings, ensuring that penal acts of worship like kaffarah are not imposed when there is genuine uncertainty. Understanding this maxim helps you grasp the broader Hanafi approach to legal obligations: certainty is required for penal punishments.

Guided Path
1
Internalize the maxim
Memorize the Arabic text: إِنَّ الْكَفَّارَةَ تَنْدَرِئُ بِالشُّبُهَاتِ. Understand its literal meaning: 'Indeed, expiation is warded off by doubts.'
The term 'tandari‘u' implies prevention or deflection.
2
Identify the legal context
This maxim is from Kitab al-Sawm (Book of Fasting). It applies to all cases where kaffarah is potentially obligatory, such as intentional breaking of a Ramadan fast or breaking an oath.
Expiation is a penal worship, so the principle of aversion with doubt is a mercy.
3
Apply the maxim to a scenario
Consider a person who mistakenly thinks they broke their fast but later doubts. Because of the doubt, kaffarah is not required. Only when the act is certain and all conditions are met does kaffarah become wajib.
This aligns with the broader Hanafi principle of avoiding penal acts in doubtful situations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إِنَّ الْكَفَّارَةَ تَنْدَرِئُ بِالشُّبُهَاتِ
Indeed, expiation is warded off by doubts.
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This maxim from Kitāb al-Ṣawm (Book of Fasting) states that if there is a doubt (shubhah) regarding the obligation of expiation (kaffārah) for a particular act, the expiation is not imposed. This is a principle of leniency: penal expiations are avoided when there is uncertainty. For example, if there is doubt whether an act actually invalidates the fast, kaffārah is not required.

Key Terms
الْكَفَّارَةَ (al-kaffārah): Expiation, a penal act of worship to atone for certain sins or violations.
الشُّبْهَة (al-shubhah): Doubt, uncertainty about a legal ruling or fact that leads to leniency.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the maxim, if there is doubt about the need for kaffārah, it is:
A) Still required
B) Warded off
C) Doubled
D) Deferred

Key Takeaway
When doubt exists regarding the obligation of expiation, the expiation is not required — certainty is needed for penal acts.

Forgetfulness Considered Based on Form
Legal maxim on the effect of forgetfulness in acts of worship.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi maxim on forgetfulness and apply it to determine whether an act invalidates an act of worship.

Core Idea
If an act naturally signals its prohibition, forgetfulness does not excuse it.

This lesson introduces a key Hanafi principle from the fiqh of fasting. The maxim evaluates whether forgetfulness can be an excuse for violating a rule. The deciding factor is the form (hay’ah) of the act: if it is inherently reminder-like (mudhakkir), forgetfulness is not considered; if not, forgetfulness may excuse. Understanding this distinction is essential for determining when an act breaks the fast.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act
Determine the specific act done forgetfully during worship (e.g., eating, using miswak, rinsing mouth).
2
Assess the hay’ah (form)
Is the act’s form such that it naturally reminds a person of the prohibition? For example, eating is obviously breaking the fast; using a miswak is not.
The hay’ah is judged by general custom and common knowledge.
3
Apply the maxim
If the hay’ah is mudhakkir (reminder-like), forgetfulness is not an excuse; the act invalidates the worship. If not mudhakkir, forgetfulness is accepted, and the act does not invalidate.
This applies across acts of worship, not only fasting.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إِنَّ الْهَيْئَةَ إِنْ كَانَتْ مُذَكَّرَةً لَا يُعْتَبَرُ النِّسْيَانُ وَإِلَّا فَهُوَ مُعْتَبَرٌ
Indeed, the form: if it is reminder-like, forgetfulness is not considered; otherwise, it is considered.
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This maxim from the chapter on what necessitates qadā' and kaffārah explains that if the physical form of an act (hay’ah) is such that it naturally reminds a person of the ruling (e.g., eating during fasting is obviously breaking the fast), then forgetfulness is not an excuse. But if the form is not inherently reminder-like (e.g., using a miswak which is not typically associated with breaking fast), then forgetfulness may be considered a valid excuse. In other words, if the act itself is a strong indicator of prohibition, ignorance due to forgetfulness is not accepted.

Key Terms
الْهَيْئَةَ (al-hay’ah): The form, state, or nature of an act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the maxim, if the form of an act is reminder-like (mudhakkirat), forgetfulness is:
A) Considered an excuse
B) Not considered
C) Considered only in Ramadan
D) Considered for women

Key Takeaway
The hay’ah (form) of an act determines whether forgetfulness excuses its commission during an act of worship.

Kaffarah of Fasting Depends on Completeness of Offense
Legal maxim on the expiation for breaking fast.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the maxim governing kaffarah for breaking fast and apply it to determine when kaffarah is required.

Core Idea
Kaffarah is only due when the offense of breaking the fast is complete.

This lesson presents a concise legal maxim from the Hanafi school regarding the expiation (kaffarah) for intentionally breaking a fast. The central idea is that not every act that breaks the fast triggers kaffarah; only a complete violation does. Understanding this maxim helps distinguish between cases requiring qadā' alone and those requiring kaffarah.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the Maxim
Learn the exact Arabic wording and its translation: 'Indeed, the expiation of fasting revolves around the completeness of the offense.'
2
Understand 'Completeness of the Offense'
The key term 'kamāl al-janāyah' means that the act must fully break the fast in a deliberate manner. For example, swallowing food or drink is a complete act; merely tasting without swallowing is not.
3
Apply to Scenarios
Take a scenario and check if the violation is complete. If the person performs the full act (e.g., swallows), kaffarah is wajib. If the act is interrupted or incomplete (e.g., spits out before swallowing), only qadā' is needed.
This distinction is unique to Hanafi fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إِنَّ كَفَّارَةَ الصَّوْمِ تَدُورُ عَلَى كَمَال الْجَنَايَةِ
Indeed, the expiation of fasting revolves around the completeness of the offense.
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This maxim from the book of fasting (ṣawm) states that the full expiation (kaffārah) for intentionally breaking a fast is due only when the offense is complete (kamāl al-janāyah). For example, if a person eats but does not complete the act (e.g., only swallows a small thing that does not break the fast fully), expiation is not required. The offense must fully violate the fast.

Key Terms
كَمَال الْجَنَايَةِ (kamāl al-janāyah): The completion of the violation; the act must fully break the fast in a deliberate, complete manner.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the maxim, kaffarah for breaking fast is required only when:
A) The fast is already broken
B) The offense is complete
C) The person is an adult
D) The person eats twice

Key Takeaway
Kaffarah for breaking fast is only required when the violation is complete; incomplete violations only require qadā'.

Legal Maxim on Nullification of the Fast
Legal maxim defining nullifier of the fast.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi legal maxim for what nullifies the fast and apply it to common scenarios.

Core Idea
The fast breaks when any nutritious substance reaches the internal cavity.

This lesson presents a concise legal maxim from the Hanafi school about the fast of Ramadan. It establishes a core principle: the fast is invalidated by the arrival of any substance that nourishes or benefits the body into the internal cavity (jawf). Understanding this maxim is essential for determining which actions nullify the fast.

Guided Path
1
Identify the elements of the maxim
Break the sentence into three parts: (1) arrival (wuṣūl), (2) what contains benefit for the body (mā fīhi ṣalāḥ al-badan), and (3) into the internal cavity (ilā al-jawf). The result: it breaks the fast (yuftiru al-ṣawm).
The maxim is a complete legal condition.
2
Define the key terms
Ṣalāḥ al-badan means anything that nourishes or benefits the body, not limited to typical food or drink. Al-jawf refers to the internal cavity, primarily the stomach or intestines.
3
Apply the maxim to typical cases
Food, drink, and medicine that reach the stomach through the mouth or nose break the fast. Mere tasting or smelling does not break it because the substance does not reach the jawf.
Intent is irrelevant; even accidental ingestion breaks the fast.
4
Distinguish from non-breaking actions
Actions like applying kohl, eating through the skin, or inhaling dust do not break the fast because the substance either does not reach the jawf or does not contain ṣalāḥ al-badan.
These are discussed in detailed fiqh texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إِنَّ وُصُولَ مَا فِيهِ صَلَاحُ الْبَدَن إِلَى الْجَوْفِ يُفْطِرُ الصَّومَ
Indeed, the arrival of what contains benefit for the body into the internal cavity breaks the fast.
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This maxim defines what causes the fast to be invalid: any substance that is nutritious for the body (mā fīhi ṣalāḥ al-badan) entering the internal cavity (jawf, essentially the stomach or equivalent) through an open passage. This includes food, drink, and medicine. The principle is that the fast is broken by the entry of anything that nourishes the body.

Key Terms
صَلَاحُ الْبَدَن (ṣalāḥ al-badan): What is beneficial or nourishing for the body.
الْجَوْفِ (al-jawf): The internal cavity of the body, primarily the stomach or intestines.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the maxim, what condition must be met for the fast to break?
A) The substance must be food or drink.
B) The substance must reach the internal cavity.
C) The act must be intentional.
D) The substance must be swallowed.

Key Takeaway
The fast is nullified when any nutritious substance reaches the internal cavity.

Obligation of Qada is Deferred
Legal maxim on the timing of making up missed fasts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on the timing of qada for missed fasts and apply it to examples, including the exception for delay until the next Ramadan.

Core Idea
The obligation to make up missed fasts is deferred, not immediate.

This lesson introduces a foundational Hanafi maxim: the obligation to perform qada (making up missed acts of worship) is not immediate but may be postponed. This maxim is especially relevant for missed Ramadan fasts, allowing flexibility. However, if delay leads to the next Ramadan without excuse, additional compensation (fidya) becomes required.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the maxim
Recite the Arabic: 'إِنَّ وُجُوبَ الْقَضَاءِ عَلَى التَّرَاخِي' and understand its literal meaning: 'Indeed, the obligation of making up is on postponement.'
2
Identify the applied ruling
The maxim applies to any missed obligatory act, especially sawm. The obligation to make up is at leisure (tarakhi) – you may delay but must eventually perform it.
The obligation is wajib, but its performance is not immediate (fawr).
3
Recognize the exception
If you delay qada of a Ramadan fast until the next Ramadan without a valid excuse, you must also pay fidya (feeding a poor person for each day missed) in addition to making up the fast.
This exception prevents indefinite postponement.
4
Apply to a scenario
Example: A person missed a fast due to travel. They can make it up any day after Ramadan, e.g., in Shawwal. But if they delay without excuse until the next Ramadan, fidya becomes due.
The maxim provides ease (taysir) in worship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إِنَّ وُجُوبَ الْقَضَاءِ عَلَى التَّرَاخِي
Indeed, the obligation of making up (missed acts) is on postponement.
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This maxim means that the obligation to perform qadā’ (making up missed obligatory acts like fasts or prayers) is not required immediately but may be delayed. For example, if a person misses a day of Ramadan due to travel, he can make it up later in the year at his convenience. However, if he delays until the next Ramadan, he must also pay fidya (compensation) if he delayed without excuse.

Key Terms
الْقَضَاءِ (al-qadā’): Making up a missed obligatory act of worship.
التَّرَاخِي (al-tarākhī): Postponement, delay; the principle that obligation does not demand immediate performance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the maxim, the obligation of qadā’ (making up) is:
A) Immediate
B) Deferred
C) Not required
D) Only for prayers

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi rule: qada of missed fasts is wajib but its performance is at leisure (tarakhi), not immediate.

Condition for Substitute is Persistence of Inability
Legal maxim on substitution in worship

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi maxim on substitution in worship and apply it to distinguish between permanent and temporary inability in fasting.

Core Idea
The substitute is only valid if the original remains incapable permanently.

In Hanafi fiqh, substitution (khalīfah) in acts of worship is allowed under specific conditions. This maxim encapsulates a key condition: the original person's inability to perform the act must be continuous and permanent. Understanding this condition is crucial to correctly applying rulings on compensation (fidya) in fasting and similar acts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
Recognize that this maxim addresses substitution (khalīfah) in worship, particularly in fasting (sawm). The ruling is that substitution is permissible only when a specific condition is met.
2
Understand the condition
The condition is 'continuance of inability' (istimrār al-‘ajz). This means the original person's inability to perform the act (e.g., fast) must be permanent and ongoing until death or indefinitely.
Temporary inability does not satisfy this condition.
3
Apply to an example
Consider a person with a chronic, incurable illness that prevents fasting permanently. They can appoint a substitute to feed a poor person for each missed day (fidya). Contrast with a person with a temporary illness who recovers; they must make up the fast later and cannot use a substitute.
The maxim ensures substitution does not replace an obligation when the original obligation can still be fulfilled.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إِنَّ شَرْطَ الْخَلِيْفَةِ اِسْتِمُرَارُ الْعِجْزِ
Indeed, the condition of the substitute is the continuance of inability.
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This maxim explains that for one person to act on behalf of another (khalīfah) in an act of worship, the inability of the original person must be continuous (istimrār al-‘ajz). For example, a person who is unable to fast due to chronic illness can delegate compensation (fidya) to feed the poor, but if the inability is temporary, he must make up the fast himself. The substitute is only allowed when the original remains incapable until death.

Key Terms
الْخَلِيْفَةِ (al-khalīfah): Substitute or deputy who performs a duty on behalf of another.
اسْتِمُرَارُ الْعِجْزِ (istimrār al-‘ajz): Continuance of inability – the state of being permanently and continuously unable to perform an act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A person with a permanent illness misses Ramadan fasts. According to the Hanafi maxim, what is the condition for a valid substitute?
A) The substitute must be a relative
B) The inability must be continuous (permanent)
C) The person must have paid zakat first
D) The substitute must fast on behalf of the person

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi maxim for substitution in worship: the substitute is only valid if the original person's inability is permanent and ongoing.

Cause of Obligation in Prayer and Fasting
Legal maxim on cause of obligation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the sabab al-wujub for prayer and fasting, and explain how it impacts the timing of intention (niyyah).

Core Idea
The obligation of prayer arises at its time; for fasting it begins at dawn—intention must precede the fast.

In Hanafi fiqh, each act of worship has a specific cause (sabab) that triggers its obligation. This short maxim from Hidayah distinguishes between prayer and fasting: for prayer, the obligation attaches at the time of performance (e.g., entry of Zuhr time), while for fasting, it attaches from the very first part of the day (dawn). This difference affects when one must make intention: for fasting, intention must be made before dawn (except for voluntary fasts), whereas for prayer, intention can be made at the start of the prayer time.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two acts mentioned
The maxim mentions salat (prayer) and sawm (fasting). Note that each has a different cause of obligation.
2
Understand the phrase for prayer
For prayer, the sabab is 'al-juz' al-muttasil bi al-ada' – the part connected to performance, i.e., the time of the prayer. This means the obligation attaches when the time enters.
The time is a condition for obligation, not the entire day.
3
Understand the phrase for fasting
For fasting, the sabab is 'al-juz' al-awwal – the first part. This means the obligation attaches from the very beginning of the day (dawn).
This is why intention for a wajib fast must be made before dawn.
4
Apply to intention (niyyah)
For prayer, one can make intention at the start of the prayer time. For fasting, the intention must be made at night or before dawn, because the obligation has already begun.
Voluntary fasts have more flexibility; this maxim applies to obligatory fasts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إِنَّ سَبَبَ الْوُجُوبِ فِى الصَّلَاةِ الْجُزْءُ الْمُتَّصِلُ بِالْأَدَاءِ وَفِي الصَّوْمِ الْجُزْءُ الْأَوَّلُ
Indeed, the cause of obligation in prayer is the part connected to performance, and in fasting, the first part.
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This maxim distinguishes the cause (sabab) of obligation for two different acts. For prayer (ṣalāh), the obligation becomes due at the time of performance (the prayer time). For fasting (ṣawm), the obligation is established from the first part of time (the beginning of the day: dawn). This affects rulings on intention (niyyah): for fasting, the intention must be made before dawn (except for voluntary fasts), whereas for prayer, intention can be made at the start of the prayer time.

Key Terms
سَبَبَ الْوُجُوبِ (sabab al-wujūb): The legal cause that triggers the obligation.
الْجُزْءُ الْمُتَّصِلُ بِالْأَدَاءِ (al-juz' al-muttasil bi al-ada'): The part connected to performance; i.e., the time slot of prayer.
الْجُزْءُ الْأَوَّلُ (al-juz' al-awwal): The first part; i.e., the beginning of the day (dawn) for fasting.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the maxim, the cause of obligation for fasting is:
A) The entire day
B) The first part (al-juz’ al-awwal)
C) Sunset
D) The time of performance

Key Takeaway
The sabab al-wujub differs between prayer and fasting: prayer's cause is its time, fasting's cause is the very start of the day, which dictates when intention must be made.

Travel Does Not Affect Obligation or Validity of Worship
Legal maxim on travel's effect on worship

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the meaning of the maxim 'travel does not affect capacity for obligation nor validity of commencement' and apply it to examples in salah and sawm.

Core Idea
Travel does not remove your obligation to worship, nor does it invalidate an act of worship you begin.

This passage presents a foundational Hanafi maxim that clarifies the legal position of a traveler (musāfir) regarding worship. It assures that the traveler remains fully obligated (ahliyyat al-wujūb) and that any act of worship initiated during travel is valid (ṣaḥīḥ). This maxim governs the application of concessions (rukhaṣ) such as shortening prayer (qasr) and breaking fast (ifṭār).

Guided Path
1
Identify the two claims
The maxim asserts that travel does not negate two things: (1) the legal capacity to be obligated (ahliyyat al-wujūb), and (2) the validity of commencing an act of worship (ṣiḥḥat al-shurū‘).
These are distinct: capacity to be obligated refers to the state of being subject to divine commands; validity of commencement refers to whether starting the act is legally sound.
2
Understand the negation
The phrase 'lā yunāfī' (does not contradict) means travel does not remove or invalidate either of these two aspects.
Travel may affect other matters (like completion of prayer or permissibility of breaking fast), but not the core obligation or the validity of inception.
3
Consider the implication for obligations
Since travel does not affect capacity for obligation, the traveler is still required to perform all obligatory (fard) acts, such as the five daily prayers and fasting in Ramadan, though concessions may modify how they are performed.
The same applies to other obligations like zakāh or ḥajj; travel does not suspend them.
4
Distinguish concessions from nullification
Concessions (rukhaṣ) such as shortening prayer, combining prayers, or breaking fast do not indicate that travel voids the obligation or invalidates the worship. Rather, they are facilitations while the obligation remains binding.
For example, a traveler who breaks his fast must still make it up (qaḍā). The fast was valid when started; the break is a separate concession.
5
Apply to specific acts
Apply the maxim to ṣalāh: a traveler who begins prayer (even if he shortens it) has a valid prayer. Apply to ṣawm: a traveler who starts a fast has a valid fast, even if he later breaks it.
These applications are fundamental to understanding how concessions interact with the original obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إِنَّ السَّفَرَ لَا يُنَافِى أَهْلِيَّةَ الْوُجُوْبِ وَلَا صِحَّةَ الشَّرُوعِ
Indeed, travel does not contradict the capacity for obligation nor the validity of commencement.
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This maxim means that being a traveler (musāfir) does not remove one's legal capacity to be obligated (ahliyyat al-wujūb) nor does it affect the validity of initiating an act of worship (ṣiḥḥat al-shurū‘). For example, a traveler is still obligated to pray, though he may shorten or combine prayers. Similarly, if a person starts a fast while traveling, the fast is valid even though he may be allowed to break it.

Key Terms
أَهْلِيَّةَ الْوُجُوْبِ (ahliyyat al-wujūb): The legal capacity to be subject to obligations (as opposed to capacity for performance).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the maxim, travel does not negate:
A) The reward of worship
B) The capacity to be obligated
C) The need for wuḍū’
D) The penalty for skipping

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi maxim affirms that travel does not remove the obligation to worship nor invalidate the act of worship when begun; concessions are facilitations, not negations.

Sawm — Fasting
beginner
6 min
Intention Required for Each Day of Fasting
Legal maxim on intention for fasting.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule that each day of fasting requires a separate intention and identify the exception for voluntary fasts.

Core Idea
Each day of fasting requires its own intention — one cannot cover a month with a single niyyah.

This maxim is a foundational rule for the validity of fasting in the Hanafi school. It clarifies that the intention (niyyah) must be renewed for each day, whether the fast is obligatory or voluntary. Understanding this rule ensures that your fasts are valid according to the school.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim in Arabic and English
Recite the maxim: لَا بُدَّ مِنْ نِيَّةِ الصَّوْمِ لِكُلِّ يَوْمٍ عَلَى حِدَةٍ — 'There must be intention of fasting for each day separately.' This is the core legal rule.
2
Identify the legal nature of intention
Intention (niyyah) is a condition (shart) for the validity of each fast. Without it, the fast is invalid (batil).
In Hanafi fiqh, intention is a condition, not a pillar.
3
Apply the rule to Ramadan (obligatory fasts)
For Ramadan, the intention must be made each night before dawn (fajr). One intention for the entire month does not suffice.
Some later Hanafi texts mention that for Ramadan, the intention of the first day can suffice if there is no interruption, but the relied-upon view is daily renewal.
4
Note the exception for voluntary (nafl) fasts
For voluntary fasts, the intention can be made even after dawn, as long as nothing has been consumed. This is a concession to encourage voluntary worship.
This exception is specific to nafl fasts; for obligatory fasts, intention must be before dawn.
5
Understand the consequence of missing intention
If a person forgets to make intention for a day of Ramadan, that day's fast is invalid and must be made up (qada).
No expiation (kaffarah) is required unless the fast was broken intentionally.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لَا بُدَّ مِنْ نِيَّةِ الصَّوْمِ لِكُلِّ يَوْمٍ عَلَى حِدَةٍ
There must be intention of fasting for each day separately.
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This maxim states that for each day of fasting (both obligatory and voluntary), a separate intention (niyyah) is required. The intention cannot cover multiple days at once. For Ramadan, one intention for the entire month is insufficient; the intention must be renewed each night before dawn. However, Hanafis have a rule that for Ramadan, the intention made for the first day suffices for the rest if there is no interruption, but the stronger view is that renewal is needed.

Key Terms
نِيَّةِ الصَّوْمِ (niyyat al-ṣawm): The intention to fast, a condition for validity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the maxim, how many intentions are required for the month of Ramadan?
A) One for the entire month
B) One for each day
C) One for the first ten days
D) None if you knew it is Ramadan

Key Takeaway
Each day of fasting requires its own intention; for Ramadan, renew it before dawn daily.

Unconsciousness Weakens Responsibility, Not Intellect
Legal maxim on effect of unconsciousness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the effect of unconsciousness (ighmā’) on legal responsibility and apply it to distinguish when qadā’ (makeup) of prayers is required.

Core Idea
Unconsciousness weakens responsibility but does not remove intellect.

This lesson presents a concise Hanafi maxim: unconsciousness only weakens the 'strong' (full legal responsibility) but does not remove the intellect (ḥijā). Understanding this principle is key to determining when missed religious duties, such as prayers, must be made up. It draws a clear line between temporary unconsciousness and permanent insanity.

Guided Path
1
Parse the maxim
Identify the two clauses: 'yuḍ‘if al-qawī faqaṭ' (weakens the strong only) and 'lā yuzīl al-ḥijā' (does not remove the intellect).
The word 'al-qawī' refers to full legal capacity; 'al-ḥijā' means intellect or reason.
2
Understand 'weakens'
Explain that unconsciousness reduces but does not nullify responsibility. For example, a brief fainting spell may exempt from making up prayers, but prolonged unconsciousness still requires qadā’.
The 'weakening' is a reduction, not a complete lifting.
3
Distinguish from insanity
Compare with junūn (insanity), which removes intellect entirely and lifts all responsibility. Unconsciousness preserves the underlying intellect.
This distinction is crucial in Hanafi fiqh for determining obligations.
4
Apply to a case
Consider a person unconscious for a short period during prayer time. According to the maxim, since intellect is not removed, the prayer obligation remains, but the 'weakening' may mean qadā’ is not required if unconscious for less than a full day?
The exact duration is determined by other principles; the maxim provides the general rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إِنَّ الْإِغْمَاءَ يُضَعِفُ الْقَوِيَّ فَقَط وَلَا يُزِيلُ الْحِجَى
Indeed, unconsciousness only weakens the strong and does not remove the intellect.
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This maxim explains the effect of temporary unconsciousness (ighmā’) on legal capacity. It weakens the 'strong' (al-qawī), meaning full legal responsibility is reduced but not eliminated. It does not remove 'aql (intellect) entirely, so someone who is unconscious may still be considered legally accountable to some degree. For example, if a person becomes unconscious for a short period, prayers missed during that time may not have to be made up, but if conscious, they do. The principle helps determine when qadā’ is required.

Key Terms
الْإِغْمَاءَ (al-ighmā’): Unconsciousness or fainting, as distinct from insanity or sleep.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the maxim, unconsciousness (ighmā’) does which of the following?
A) Removes all responsibility
B) Only weakens the strong but does not remove intellect
C) Cancels all contracts
D) Invalidates all worship

Key Takeaway
Unconsciousness weakens legal responsibility but never removes the intellect, distinguishing it from insanity.

Expiation Attaches to Invalidating, Not Abstaining
Legal maxim on expiation for breaking fast.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi maxim on expiation and distinguish between invalidation (ifsād) and abstention (imtināʿ) in the context of fasting.

Core Idea
Expiation (kaffārah) is due only when you actively break a valid fast, not merely for not fasting.

This lesson presents a foundational Hanafi maxim that distinguishes between two scenarios: one who never starts a fast (imtināʿ) and one who starts then intentionally invalidates it (ifsād). Understanding this distinction is critical for correctly applying the ruling of kaffārah in Ramaḍān.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim in Arabic and English.
Memorize the Arabic text: إِنَّ الْكَفَّارَةَ تَعَلَّقَتْ بِالْإِفْسَادِ لَا بِالْاِمْتِنَاعِ. The English: 'Indeed, expiation is attached to invalidating, not to refraining.'
This is a concise legal principle (qaʿidah fiqhiyyah).
2
Define the two key terms: ifsād and imtināʿ.
ifsād means actively invalidating a valid act in progress (e.g., eating or drinking while already in a fast). imtināʿ means refraining or abstaining entirely—here, not beginning the fast at all.
The contrast is between 'breaking' and 'not starting'.
3
Apply the maxim to a concrete scenario.
If a person does not begin a fast on a day of Ramaḍān without a valid excuse, they commit a sin but owe only qaḍāʾ (make up). If they begin the fast and then intentionally eat or drink, they owe both qaḍāʾ and kaffārah.
kaffārah is an additional penalty beyond qaḍāʾ.
4
Note the exception: what if a valid excuse exists?
If one has a sharʿī excuse (like illness or travel) for not fasting, neither qaḍāʾ nor kaffārah is immediately due—only qaḍāʾ later. The maxim addresses the unexcussed case.
The maxim operates on the basis that the fast was obligatory and validly started.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إِنَّ الْكَفَّارَةَ تَعَلَّقَتْ بِالْإِفْسَادِ لَا بِالْاِمْتِنَاعِ
Indeed, expiation is attached to invalidating, not to refraining.
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This maxim clarifies that expiation (kaffārah) for breaking an obligatory fast is due only when one actively invalidates a fast that has been validly started. It is not due for simply not fasting (imtinā‘) from the beginning. For example, if someone does not start a fast at all (without excuse), they only need to make it up (qadā’), but if they start and then intentionally break it, they require both qadā’ and kaffārah.

Key Terms
الْإِفْسَادِ (al-ifsād): Invalidation of a valid act in progress.
الْاِمْتِنَاعِ (al-imtināʿ): Abstention or refraining from beginning the act (here, not starting the fast).
الْكَفَّارَةَ (al-kaffārah): Expiation; a specific penalty for invalidating a Ramadan fast intentionally.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the maxim, kaffārah is due for:
A) Not fasting
B) Invalidating a valid fast
C) Forgetting to fast
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school teaches that kaffārah is a penalty for actively breaking a validly started Ramaḍān fast, not for failing to start it.

Obligation of Qada is Based on Obligation of Performance
Legal maxim on qada obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi maxim on qada and apply it to determine whether a missed act requires make-up.

Core Idea
Qada depends on original obligation: if an act was not wajib, no qada.

This short maxim is a foundational principle in Hanafi fiqh. It links the duty of making up (qada) a missed worship to the original duty of performing it (ada). Understanding this maxim prevents confusion between obligatory and voluntary acts.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the maxim
Commit the Arabic to memory: 'إِنَّ وُجُوبَ الْقَضَاءِ يَبْتَنِي عَلَى وُجُوبِ الْقِيَامِ' (note: some manuscripts have القيانة, but the correct is القيام meaning performance).
The word qiyam here means ada (performance).
2
Identify the two key terms
wujūb al-qadā’ (obligation of making up) and wujūb al-qiyām (obligation of performance). The maxim states that the first is built upon the second.
3
Apply the maxim to a case
Given a missed act, ask: Was this act originally obligatory (fard or wajib)? If yes, qada is required. If no (voluntary), no qada.
Be careful: some voluntary acts become wajib after starting (e.g., breaking a nafl fast without excuse).
4
Recognize exceptions
Acts exempted by concession (rukhsa) – such as missed prayers during travel for a certain time – may not require qada. But the general rule holds.
Seek further rulings for specific concessions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إِنَّ وُجُوبَ الْقَضَاءِ يَبْتَنِي عَلَى وُجُوبِ القِيَانَةِ
Indeed, the obligation of making up is based on the obligation of standing (performance).
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This maxim states that the obligation to make up (qadā’) a missed act of worship depends on the original obligation to perform it (qiyām). If an act was never obligatory (e.g., voluntary), qadā’ is not required. For example, if someone misses a Salat due to sleep, qadā’ is required because the original obligation existed. If a voluntary fast is broken, qadā’ is not required by some views.

Key Terms
وُجُوبَ الْقَضَاءِ (wujūb al-qadā’): The obligation to make up a missed obligatory act.
الْقِيَامِ (al-qiyām): Performance (ada) of an act; standing in worship.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the maxim, the obligation to make up (qadā’) is based on:
A) The person's ability
B) The original obligation to perform
C) The type of worship
D) The person's health

Key Takeaway
A missed act requires make-up only if it was originally obligatory (fard or wajib).

The Foundation of I'tikaf: Consecutiveness vs. Fasting's Flexibility
Legal maxim on i'tikaf and fasting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the difference between i'tikaf and fasting regarding consecutiveness, and apply it to determine the validity of a broken vowed i'tikaf.

Core Idea
I'tikaf requires consecutiveness (tatābu‘) by nature; fasting does not.

This short maxim from the Hanafi tradition captures a fundamental structural difference between two acts of worship. Understanding this difference is crucial for correctly performing vowed i'tikaf and for making up missed fasts. The maxim is often cited to explain why breaking a vowed i'tikaf requires restarting from the beginning, while missed fasts can be made up on separate days.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two acts
The maxim mentions i'tikaf (seclusion in the mosque for worship) and fasting (sawm). Recognize that both are acts of worship but with different structural requirements.
2
Contrast their foundations
I'tikaf is built on consecutiveness (tatābu‘), meaning it must be performed on consecutive days without break. Fasting is built on separation (tafrīq), meaning it can be performed on non-consecutive days.
This is a general rule; voluntary i'tikaf of even one day is valid, but vowed i'tikaf requires consecutiveness.
3
Apply to vowed i'tikaf
If someone vows ten days of i'tikaf but breaks it after three, the consecutiveness is lost. According to the Hanafi school, the entire ten days must be restarted from the beginning.
Breaking here means leaving the mosque without a valid excuse or for a significant period.
4
Apply to fasting
Missed Ramadan fasts can be made up on any days, consecutively or non-consecutively. Voluntary fasting also does not require consecutiveness.
However, fasting on consecutive days is permissible and often recommended for certain acts like the six days of Shawwal.
5
Distinguish voluntary vs. obligatory
Voluntary i'tikaf (sunnah) can be for any period, even one day, without requiring consecutiveness beyond that day. Only a vowed (wajib) i'tikaf must be consecutive if specified as such in the vow.
If the vow does not specify consecutiveness, Hanafis still require it due to the nature of i'tikaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إِنَّ مَبْنَى الْإِعْتِكَافِ عَلَى التَتابع بِخِلَافِ الصَّوْمِ فَإِنَّ مَبْنَاهُ عَلَى الْتَفْرِقِ
Indeed, the foundation of i‘tikāf is upon consecutiveness, contrary to fasting, for its foundation is upon separation (non-consecutive).
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This maxim explains a key difference between i‘tikāf and fasting. I‘tikāf (seclusion in the mosque for worship) must be performed on consecutive days if vowed; if interrupted, the entire duration must be restarted. Fasting (ṣawm), on the other hand, can be done non-consecutively— for example, making up missed Ramadan fasts on separate days. However, voluntary fasting can also be consecutive.

Key Terms
التَتابع (al-tatābu‘): Consecutiveness, without break between days.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the maxim, i‘tikāf is built on:
A) Consecutiveness
B) Non-consecutiveness
C) Alternating with fasting
D) None

Key Takeaway
I'tikaf inherently requires consecutiveness (tatābu‘), while fasting does not, leading to different rulings when an interruption occurs.

Kaffarah Dropped by Doubts in Moon Sighting: A Legal Analysis
Applying kaffarah-dropping principle to solitary moon sighting case

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on kaffarah when a single moon sighting is rejected by a judge, and apply the principle that kaffarah is averted by doubts (shubuhat).

Core Idea
Kaffarah requires certainty; doubt from a judge's rejection drops it.

Imagine you are the only person in your town who saw the Ramadan crescent. You report it, but the judge rejects your testimony due to suspicion of error. Later, you break your fast. Are you required to perform kaffarah? This lesson explores the Hanafi answer: qada is wajib, but kaffarah is not, because kaffarah is averted by doubts. We will examine the legal reasoning and evidence from the Qur'an and Sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
A person claims to have sighted the Ramadan moon alone. The judge rejects his testimony due to tuhmat al-ghalat (suspicion of error).
The judge acts on a valid legal reason, not arbitrarily.
2
Recognize the Legal Doubt
The rejection creates a shubhah (doubt) about whether the day is actually Ramadan.
The doubt is objective, not just personal uncertainty.
3
Apply the Principle: Al-Kafarah Tandari' bi al-Shubuhat
Kaffarah is averted by doubts. Since a doubt exists, kaffarah is not obligatory.
This is a well-established Hanafi maxim.
4
Examine the Evidences
The Qur'an addresses the month to the community (the 'you' is plural). The Prophet (PBUH) said: 'Your fast is the day you (all) fast.' So individual certitude does not establish Ramadan for the community, and thus doubt remains.
The proofs establish that the month is attached to the generality.
5
Derive the Ruling
Qada (make-up fast) is wajib because the person still had a duty to fast based on his own sighting. But kaffarah is not wajib because its application requires certainty, which is absent.
This is the mufta bihi Hanafi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن القاضي رد شهادته بدلیل شرعی وهو تهمة الغلط لأن تفرده بالرؤية مع تساوى كافة الناس في النظر والمنظر والجو والالتماس يورث تهمة الغلط وهذه الكفارة تندرء بالشبهات ولأن عدم وجوب الصوم على غيره دليل على أن هذا اليوم ليس من رمضان في حق الكافة وكذا في حقه لقوله تعالى : فَمَنْ شَهِدَ مِنْكُمُ الشَّهْرَ فَلْيَصُمُهُ. وقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم: صوموا شهركم. وقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم: صومكم يوم تصومون. فجعل الشهر مضافا إلى الكافة لا إلى واحد بعينه فلا تثبت الرمضانية إلا بوجوب الصوم على الكل فإذا لم تثبت الرمضانية قطعا لا تجب الكفارة.
الغرة المنيفة في تحقيق بعض مسائل الإمام أبي حنيفة: كتاب الصوم، ص ٦٤، ط: المؤسسة للكتب الثقافية
That the judge rejected his testimony due to a legal reason, which is the suspicion of error, because his being alone in sighting while all other people are equal in vision, sight, atmosphere, and search gives rise to suspicion of error. And this kaffarah is averted by doubts. And because the non-obligation of fasting on others is evidence that this day is not from Ramadan with respect to everyone, and similarly with respect to him, due to His saying: 'So whoever among you witnesses the month, let him fast it.' And his saying (peace be upon him): 'Fast your month.' And his saying: 'Your fast is the day you fast.' So the month is made specific to the generality, not to a particular individual. Thus Ramadan is not established except by the obligation of fasting on all. So when Ramadan is not established definitively, kaffarah is not obligatory.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof. It explains the principle 'al-kafarah tandari' bi al-shubuhat' (kaffarah is averted by doubts). The specific case is a person who alone sights the Ramadan moon but the qadi (judge) rejects his testimony due to suspicion of error (tuhmat al-ghalat). The suspicion arises because everyone else in the area did not see the moon despite equal conditions. The judge's rejection creates a doubt (shubhah) about whether it is truly Ramadan. The ruling:
1. The person must still fast because the general command 'fast when you see the moon' applies to him individually.
2. If he breaks the fast (e.g., by having intercourse), qada (make-up) is wajib, but kaffarah is not, because kaffarah is dropped by the doubt introduced by the judge's rejection.
The proof includes:
- The judge acted on a legal reason (suspicion of error).
- Kaffarah is averted by doubts (a principle from hadith).
- From the Qur'an and Sunnah, the month of Ramadan is addressed to the community, not individuals. Since the day is not established as Ramadan for the community definitively, kaffarah is not due.
Thus, the principle is applied: kaffarah is severe and requires certainty; doubt removes it.

Key Terms
كفاره (kaffārah): An expiatory penalty for deliberately breaking a Ramadan fast without excuse (e.g., fasting two months or feeding sixty poor).
شبهات (shubuhāt): Doubts or uncertainties in legal matters that prevent application of hadd or kaffarah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case where a single person sights the Ramadan moon but the qadi rejects his testimony, what is the ruling regarding kaffarah if he breaks the fast?
A) Kaffarah is wajib
B) Kaffarah is not wajib due to doubt
C) Both qada and kaffarah are dropped
D) Only qada is required and kaffarah is also required

Key Takeaway
Kaffarah for breaking a Ramadan fast is only due when the day is established as Ramadan with certainty; any legal doubt (shubhah) drops the kaffarah, although qada remains wajib.

beginner
6 min
The Fasiq's Testimony in Religious Matters
States a legal maxim and applies it to moon sighting testimony

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi maxim that a fasiq's testimony is rejected in diyānāt and apply it to determine the conditions for accepting moon sighting testimony.

Core Idea
A fasiq’s word is rejected in religious matters; only a just person’s testimony counts.

This lesson introduces a foundational Hanafi legal maxim: the testimony of a fasiq (sinner) is unacceptable in all religious matters (diyānāt). The author of al-Hidayah applies this to the sighting of the Ramadan crescent, showing that when the sky is cloudy, the testimony of a single just person suffices. The key condition is 'adālah (moral integrity), not number or gender.

Guided Path
1
1. Identify the maxim and its scope
Read the Arabic maxim: 'Inna qawl al-fāsiq fī al-diyānāt ghayr maqbūl.' Understand that it applies to all religious matters, not worldly transactions. 'Diyānāt' includes acts of worship and related testimonies.
The maxim is general; no exceptions are stated for emergencies or special cases.
2
2. Recognize the condition of 'adālah
The text states that in religious matters, the witness must be 'ādil (just). 'Adālah means refraining from major sins and not persisting in minor sins. This is the main condition for accepting testimony.
Other schools may have additional conditions; Hanafi focuses on justice alone in this context.
3
3. Apply to the moon sighting scenario
The author applies the maxim to ru'yat al-hilāl. When the sky is cloudy, the imam accepts the testimony of one just person (male or female, free or slave) because it is a religious matter. The fasiq's word is excluded.
When the sky is clear, two just witnesses are required; the maxim applies only to cloudy conditions as per Hidayah.
4
4. Note the practical implication
This ruling eases moon sighting in cloudy regions. The emphasis on 'adālah ensures reliability in religious observance.
Intra-madhhab: Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad agree on this principle, though later discussions exist.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(١٦) إِنَّ قَوْلَ الْفَاسِقِ فِي الدِّيَانَاتِ غَيْرُ مَقْبُولِ.
ترجمہ: دیانات میں فاسق کا قول معتبر نہیں ہے۔
تشریح مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ دینی امور و معاملات میں فاسق کا قول معتبر نہیں ہے۔ صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے اس بات کو ثابت کر رہے ہیں کہ اگر آسمان میں علت ہو یعینی ابر وغیرہ ہو تو اس صورت میں امام چاند دیکھنے میں ایک عادل آدمی کی گواہی کو قبول کرے گا، چاہے وہ ایک عادل مرد ہو یا عورت، آزاد ہو یا غلام لیکن عدالت شرط ہے کہ ایک عادل ہو، اس کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ یہ دیانات کے قبیل سے ہے اور دیانات میں فاسق کا قول معتبر نہیں ہے بلکہ عادل ہونا ضروری ہے۔
(16) Indeed, the statement of a sinful person is not accepted in religious matters. Translation: The statement of a sinful person is not considered in religious matters. Explanation: The meaning of the aforementioned principle is that in religious affairs and transactions, the statement of a sinful person is not considered. The author of Hidayah mentions this principle to prove that if there is a cloud in the sky, i.e., clouds etc., then in that case the Imam will accept the testimony of one just person for seeing the moon, whether that just person is a man or a woman, free or slave, but justice (adalah) is a condition that he be just. The reason is that this is from the category of religious matters, and in religious matters the statement of a sinful person is not accepted; rather, it is necessary to be just.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage presents a foundational legal maxim (qā‘idah) from the Hanafi school: the testimony of a fāsiq (one who commits major sins or persists in minor sins) is rejected in all religious matters (al-diyānāt). The author applies this to the sighting of the Ramadan crescent (ru’yat al-hilāl). In Hanafi fiqh, when the sky is cloudy, the testimony of a single ‘ādil (just) person is sufficient to establish the start of Ramadan. This person may be male or female, free or slave; the essential condition is ‘adālah. The reasoning is that moon sighting is a religious matter akin to narrating hadith, where the narrator must be reliable. Thus, the fāsiq's word is categorically rejected. The principle underscores the importance of moral integrity in matters of religious observance.

Key Terms
الْفَاسِقِ (al-fāsiq): A person who commits major sins or persists in minor sins, lacking moral integrity.
الدِّيَانَاتِ (al-diyānāt): Religious matters, including rulings of ibadat and related testimonies.
الْعَدَالَةُ (al-‘adālah): The quality of being morally upright and avoiding major sins; condition for testimony acceptance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Forgetfulness in Fasting and the Hay’ah Principle
Explains a principle about forgetfulness and applies it to fasting vs prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on forgetful eating/drinking during fasting, explain the hay’ah principle that determines whether forgetfulness is excused, and evaluate the Maliki counter-argument based on qiyas.

Core Idea
Forgetfulness is excused when the act's form does not remind you of it — so fasting, not prayer.

This lesson explores a key Hanafi principle: if the outward form (hay’ah) of an act constantly reminds the doer of that act, then genuine forgetfulness cannot be claimed. The author of Al-Hidayah uses this to explain why forgetfully eating or drinking does not break the fast, contrary to the analogy with prayer. We will trace the proof from hadith and the preferred Hanafi reliance on istihsan over qiyas.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling and the principle
Identify the Hanafi position: forgetful eating/drinking does not invalidate the fast. Then define the hay’ah principle: if the act’s form reminds the doer, forgetfulness is not accepted; otherwise it is.
The principle is stated in Arabic at the beginning of the passage.
2
Identify the dalil and its wajh al-dalalah
Locate the hadith from Abu Hurairah. Note how the imperative 'فَلْيُتِمَّ' and the attribution 'أَطْعَمَهُ اللَّهُ' prove that the fast continues and that no sin or invalidation occurs.
The hadith is a sahih ahad hadith, zanni al-thubut but qati al-dalalah according to Hanafi usul.
3
Compare with the opposing qiyas
Understand the Maliki position: they analogize fasting to prayer, where any consumption or speech breaks the act. The rule that a thing is invalidated by its opposite (e.g., eating breaks abstinence) appears logical.
The opposing qiyas is based on the general principle of invalidation by contrary act.
4
Apply the hay’ah principle to distinguish fasting from prayer
Prayer has a distinct, active form (standing, bowing, prostrating) that constantly reminds the worshipper; thus forgetfulness is not plausible. Fasting is inward; the fasting person looks no different from a non-fasting person, so forgetfulness can genuinely occur.
This distinction is the core rebuttal to the qiyas.
5
Conclude: preference for istihsan over qiyas
Know that the Hanafi school gives precedence to the explicit hadith and the logic of hay’ah over the analogy. This is a classic case of istihsan overriding qiyas.
The thamarah al-khilaf is clear: Hanafis require no qada or kaffarah; Malikis require qada.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

ترجمہ: اگر ہیئت و کیفیت یاد دلانے والی ہو تو نسیان کا اعتبار نہیں ہوگا، ورنہ نسیان معتبر ہے۔

عَنْ أَبِي هُرَيْرَةَ رَضِيَ اللَّهُ عَنْهُ، عَنِ النَّبِيِّ صَلَّى اللهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ، قَالَ إِذَا نَسِيَ فَأَكَلَ وَشَرِبَ، فَلْيُتِمَّ صَوْمَهُ، فَإِنَّمَا أَطْعَمَهُ اللَّهُ وَسَقَاهُ .
ترجمہ: حضرت ابو ہریرہ رضی اللہ عنہ نے نبی کریم صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم سے روایت کی کہ آپ صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم نے فرمایا: جب کوئی بھول گیا اور کچھ کھا پی لیا تو اسے چاہئے کہ اپنا روزہ پورا کرے، کیونکہ اس کو اللہ نے کھلایا اور پلایا ہے۔
بہر حال مذکورہ حدیث سے یہ بات ثابت ہوئی کہ بھول کر کھانے پینے سے روزہ نہیں ٹوٹتا برخلاف نماز کے، کیونکہ نماز کی ہیئت انسان کو یاد دلاتی ہے، نماز کی حالت میں ہمہ وقت یاد رہتا ہے کہ میں نماز میں ہوں، کیونکہ نماز کی حالت غیر حالت نماز کے بالکل مختلف ہے، نماز میں اس قدر کثرت سے اور اد وظائف اور تسبیحات ہیں کہ نمازی ایک پل کے لئے بھی نماز کو نہیں بھول سکتا ہے، ہمہ وقت یاد رہتا ہے کہ میں نماز کی حالت میں ہوں۔ پس نماز کی حالت میں نسیان معتبر نہیں ہوگا بر خلاف روزے کا، اس کا تعلق باطن سے ہوتا ہے، اور رمضان کے علاوہ میں انسان کو کھانے پینے کی عادت رہتی ہے اس لئے روزے میں نسیان کا غالب ہونا مستبعد نہیں ہے۔ اس لئے روزے کو نماز پر قیاس کرنا درست نہیں ہے۔ امام مالک رحمہ اللہ نے روزے کو نماز پر قیاس کیا تھا، تو مذکورہ اصول اس قیاس کے جواب پر مبنی ہے۔
بخلاف الصلاة لأن هيئة الصلاة مذكرة هيئة الصلاة القيام والركوع والسجود والانتقال من واحد إلى واحد، وكل هذه الأفعال تذكر المصلى. فلا يغلب النسيان. ولا يستلزم غلبة النسيان عدم نفى هيئات ما ولا مذكر في الصوم فيغلب لأن هيئة الصائم وغير الصائم سواء لأن الصوم أمر مبطن فغلب عليه النسيان.
(128) Indeed, if the form/state is reminding, forgetfulness is not considered; otherwise, it is considered. Translation: If the form and condition is reminding, then forgetfulness will not be considered; otherwise, forgetfulness is considered. Explanation: The meaning of the aforementioned principle is that if a person is engaged in such an act that its form and condition reminds him of that act, then forgetting will not be considered; but if he is engaged in such an act that its state and condition is not reminding, then forgetfulness will be considered. The author of Hidayah mentions this principle to prove that if a fasting person forgetfully eats, drinks, or has intercourse, then by istihsan his fast will not break. Qiyas would require that the fast breaks in these cases, and that is the saying of Imam Malik (may Allah have mercy on him). The reason for qiyas is that fasting is called imsak (abstention), and eating, drinking, or intercourse is the opposite of imsak; and the rule is that a thing becomes invalid by its opposite. Therefore, in this case the fast should break, and it is like prayer, where forgetting to eat, drink, or talk breaks the prayer. Similarly, the fast would break by forgetful eating and drinking. The reason for istihsan is this hadith: From Abu Hurairah (may Allah be pleased with him) from the Prophet (peace be upon him) who said: "When someone forgets and eats or drinks, let him complete his fast, for indeed Allah has fed him and given him drink." Translation: Hadhrat Abu Hurairah (may Allah be pleased with him) narrated from the Prophet (peace be upon him) that he said: "When someone forgets and eats or drinks, let him complete his fast, for indeed Allah has fed him and given him drink." In any case, from the aforementioned hadith it is established that forgetful eating and drinking does not break the fast, contrary to prayer, because the form of prayer reminds a person; in the state of prayer, he is always mindful that he is in prayer, because the state of prayer is completely different from the non-prayer state. In prayer, there are so many movements, functions, and glorifications that the worshipper cannot forget prayer even for a moment; he is always aware that he is in the state of prayer. Therefore, forgetfulness in the state of prayer will not be considered, contrary to fasting. Fasting relates to the inner self, and outside of Ramadan, a person is accustomed to eating and drinking, so the prevalence of forgetfulness in fasting is not unlikely. Therefore, it is not correct to analogize fasting to prayer. Imam Malik (may Allah have mercy on him) analogized fasting to prayer, so the mentioned principle is based on refuting that analogy. Contrary to prayer, because the form of prayer is reminding: the form of prayer involves standing, bowing, prostrating, and moving from one to another, and all these actions remind the worshipper. So forgetfulness does not prevail. And the prevalence of forgetfulness does not necessitate the absence of any reminding forms; and there is nothing reminding in fasting, so it prevails, because the form of the fasting person and the non-fasting person is the same, because fasting is an inward matter, so forgetfulness prevails over it.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage presents a crucial usuli principle and its application in Hanafi fiqh. The principle states that if the very form (hay’ah) and condition of an act reminds the doer of that act, then unintentional forgetfulness (nisyān) is not excused; otherwise, it is excused. The author applies this to the case of forgetful eating and drinking while fasting. Hanafis rule that the fast remains valid by istihsan (juristic preference) based on a sahih hadith from Abu Hurairah: the Prophet (peace be upon him) commanded completion of the fast. The qiyas (analogy) would analogize fasting to prayer, where any such act breaks it. The Hanafi rebuttal is that the hay’ah (external form) of prayer is constantly reminding—standing, bowing, prostrating—so one cannot genuinely forget being in prayer. Fasting, being an inward state, lacks such external cues; the fasting person looks no different from a non-fasting person. Therefore, forgetfulness is plausible and excused. This illustrates the Hanafi preference for istihsan over qiyas in matters where a specific text exists. The passage also mentions the opposing view of Imam Malik, who applied qiyas.

Key Terms
الْهَيْئَةَ (al-hay’ah): The outward form, state, or condition of an act that reminds the doer of the act's nature.
النِّسْيَانُ (al-nisyān): Unintentional forgetfulness; in this context, it determines whether the act is excused.
الْقِيَاسُ (al-qiyās): Analogical reasoning; here, analogizing fasting to prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school rule that forgetfully eating or drinking does not break a fast, according to the principle in this lesson?
A) Because the fast is only broken by intentional acts.
B) Because the hay’ah (form) of fasting is not reminding, so forgetfulness is excused by hadith and istihsān.
C) Because qiyās requires that the fast breaks, but the Hanafi school rejects qiyās.
D) Because Imam Mālik said so.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school validates a fast despite forgetful eating/drinking because the non-reminding form of fasting makes genuine forgetfulness possible and the explicit hadith commands completion.

Forgetful Eating: Fasting vs Prayer – A Hanafi Distinction via Istihsan
Distinguishing fasting from prayer in forgetfulness via istihsan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on forgetful eating in fasting and explain why it differs from prayer using istihsan.

Core Idea
Forgetful eating does not break the fast because fasting is internal; prayer's physical postures prevent forgetfulness.

This passage from Al-Hidayah tackles a well-known ikhtilaf: does forgetful eating nullify the fast? The Hanafi school, relying on an explicit hadith and istihsan, holds that the fast remains valid. In contrast, Imam Malik analogized fasting to prayer, where forgetful eating invalidates. The commentary unpacks why the qiyas fails, highlighting the distinctive nature of each act.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position and its textual proof
Recall the hadith from Abu Hurairah: the Prophet instructed completion of the fast. Identify the ruling: the fast remains valid.
2
Identify the opposing view and its basis
Imam Malik analogized fasting to prayer, where forgetful eating invalidates. Understand the qiyas.
3
Examine the Hanafi response via istihsan
The commentary argues that prayer's external postures constantly remind, making forgetfulness unlikely; fasting is internal, so forgetfulness prevails. This istihsan overrides the qiyas.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: Hanafi – valid fast, no qada'; Maliki – invalid, qada' required.

From Abu Hurairah, may Allah be pleased with him, from the Prophet, peace be upon him, who said: When someone forgets and eats or drinks, let him complete his fast, for Allah has fed him and given him drink. Contrary to prayer, because the form of prayer is a reminder: the form of prayer—standing, bowing, prostration, and moving from one to another—all these actions remind the worshipper. So forgetfulness does not prevail, nor does it necessitate the absence of any forms? (Translation:... Imam Malik, may Allah have mercy on him, analogized fasting to prayer, and the mentioned principle is based on the response to this analogy.)
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage establishes that forgetful eating and drinking does not invalidate the fast, contrary to prayer. The proof is the hadith from Abu Hurairah where the Prophet instructs completion of the fast. The Hanafi school distinguishes fasting from prayer through istihsan (juristic preference): the physical postures and frequent recitations in prayer constantly remind the worshiper, making genuine forgetfulness unlikely. Fasting is an internal act; the fasting person's external state is identical to normal days, so forgetfulness is prevalent. Imam Malik analogized fasting to prayer (qiyas), but Hanafis reject this analogy because of the material difference in the nature of the acts. The principle of istihsan overrides the qiyas.

Key Terms
نسیان (nisyān): Forgetfulness that overcomes a person unintentionally.
استحسان (istiḥsān): Juristic preference that abandons a qiyas due to a stronger textual or rational consideration.
هيئة الصلاة (hay'at al-ṣalāh): The physical form and postures of prayer (standing, bowing, prostration) that serve as constant reminders.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because fasting is a personal act and prayer is communal.
D) Because the hadith only mentions fasting and not prayer.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes fasting from prayer regarding forgetfulness because of the external reminding nature of prayer, using istihsan to override the qiyas.

The Rare is Like Non-Existent: Mistake and Coercion in Fasting
Applying a qaa'idah to a fiqh dispute on fasting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the qaa'idah 'al-nadir ka'l-ma'dum' to distinguish between forgetfulness and mistake/coercion in fasting, and state the consequent Hanafi ruling of qada'.

Core Idea
The rare is legally non-existent: mistake and coercion break the fast; forgetfulness does not.

This lesson examines a foundational Hanafi legal maxim: 'The rare is treated as non-existent' (al-nadir ka'l-ma'dum). The author of Al-Hidayah uses this principle to resolve a dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i regarding whether a fasting person must make up the day if he eats or drinks by mistake or under coercion. By understanding the frequency and source of the excuse, the Hanafi school derives a ruling that differs from the Shafi'i analogy to forgetfulness.

Guided Path
1
State the qaa'idah and its meaning
Identify the maxim 'al-nadir ka'l-ma'dum' and explain that infrequent occurrences are given the legal status of nonexistence.
The maxim is used to justify treating mistake and coercion differently from forgetfulness.
2
Identify the Hanafi ruling on mistake/coercion in fasting
State that according to Hanafis, if a fasting person eats or drinks by mistake or under coercion, the fast is invalidated and qada' (make-up) is wajib.
This is the mufta bihi position from zahir al-riwayah.
3
Compare with the Shafi'i position
Note that Imam al-Shafi'i analogizes mistake and coercion to forgetfulness, which does not break the fast, so no qada' is required.
The Shafi'i analogy is based on the similarity of lacking intent.
4
Analyze the Hanafi rebuttal
Examine the two Hanafi arguments: (1) forgetfulness is frequent, mistake/coercion are rare, so the latter are treated as non-existent by the qaa'idah; (2) forgetfulness is from Allah (the right-holder), while mistake/coercion are from humans, so they differ in source.
A secondary analogy to the restrained vs. sick person in prayer qada' reinforces the distinction.
5
Conclude with the practical consequence
The thamarah al-khilaf is clear: Hanafis require qada', Shafi'is do not. The Hanafi position is mufta bihi based on the qaa'idah and the stronger distinction.
This ruling applies to both mistake (khata') and coercion (ikrah) in fasting.

(۱۶۹) إِنَّ النَّادِرَ كَالْمَعْدُومِ . ترجمہ: نادر معدوم کی طرح ہے۔ تشریح : مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ جس چیز کا وجود کم ہو وہ نہ ہونے کے حکم میں ہوتی ہے، یعنی قلیل الوقوع چیز معدوم کے حکم میں ہوتی ہے۔ صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے اس بات کو ثابت کر رہے ہیں کہ مخطئی اور مکرہ پر قضاء واجب ہے، اگر کسی روزہ دار نے غلطی سے کوئی چیز کھاپی لی یا ز بر دستی کسی نے اُسے کچھ کھلا پلا دیا تو ہمارے یہاں اس کا روزہ ٹوٹ جائے گا، اور اس پر روزے کی قضاء واجب ہے، امام شافعی رحمہ اللہ فرماتے ہیں کہ جس طرح بھول کر کھانے پینے سے روزہ نہیں ٹو شتا اسی طرح خطاء اور اکراہ میں بھی روزہ نہیں ٹوٹے گا۔ احناف فرماتے ہیں کہ نسیان اور خطاء واکراہ کے درمیان واضح فرق ہے اس لئے ایک کو دوسرے پر قیاس کرنا درست نہیں ہے، نسیان کثیر الوقوع ہے جب کہ خطاء واکراہ قلیل الوقوع ہیں، اور قلیل معدوم کے حکم میں ہوتا ہے۔ نیز نسیان صاحب حق یعنی اللہ کی طرف سے ہے اور خطاء واکراہ بندوں کی طرف سے ہے۔ (الهداية: كتاب الصوم، باب ما يوجب القضاء والكفارة، ج ۱ ص ۲۳۴، ط: رحمانيه لِأَنَّهُ لَوْ كَانَ مُخْطِئًا أَوْ مُكْرَهَا فَعَلَيْهِ الْقَضَاءِ حِلَافًا لِلشَّافِعِيِّ، فَإِنَّهُ يَعْتَبِرُ بِالنَّاسِي، وَلَنَا أَنَّهُ لَا يَغْلِبُ وُجُودَهُ وَعُذْرُ النِّسْيَانِ غَالِبٌ، وَلَأَنَّ النِّسْيَانَ مِنْ قِبَلِ مَنْ لَهُ الْحَقُّ، وَالْإِكْرَاهُ مِنْ قِبَلِ غَيْرِهِ فَيَفْتَرِقَانِ كَالْمُقَيَّدِ وَالْمَرِيضِ الْعَاجِزِ عَنْ الْآدَاءِ بِالرَّأْسِ فِي قَضَاءِ الصَّلَاةِ حَيْثُ يَقْضِي الْمُقَيَّدُ لَا الْمَرِيضُ. البحر الرائق: كتاب الصوم، باب ما يفسد الصوم وما لا يفسده، ج ۲ ص ۲۹۲، ط: دار الكتاب الإسلامي)
(169) Indeed, the rare is like the non-existent. Translation: The rare is like the non-existent. Explanation: The meaning of this principle is that something that rarely occurs is treated as non-existent, i.e., a thing of infrequent occurrence is in the ruling of non-existence. The author of Al-Hidayah mentions this principle to prove that qada' is obligatory upon the mistaken and the coerced: if a fasting person eats or drinks by mistake, or someone forces him to eat or drink, then according to us his fast breaks and he must make up the day. Imam al-Shafi'i says that just as forgetful eating/drinking does not break the fast, similarly mistake and coercion do not break it. The Hanafis say there is a clear difference between forgetfulness and mistake/coercion, so one cannot be analogized to the other: forgetfulness is frequent, while mistake and coercion are rare, and the rare is treated as non-existent. Also, forgetfulness comes from the One who has the right (Allah), while mistake and coercion come from others. (Al-Hidayah: Kitab al-Sawm, Bab ma yujibu al-qada' wa al-kaffarah, vol. 1, p. 234: 'Because if one is mistaken or coerced, he must make up, contrary to al-Shafi'i, for he analogizes to the forgetful person. For us, its occurrence is not prevalent, while the excuse of forgetfulness is prevalent, and because forgetfulness is from the One who has the right, while coercion is from another, so they differ, like the restrained person and the sick person who is unable to lead with the head in making up prayer, where the restrained person makes up but not the sick person.' Al-Bahr al-Raiq: Kitab al-Sawm, Bab ma yufsid al-sawm wa ma la yufsiduhu, vol. 2, p. 292)
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage presents a fundamental qaa'idah fiqhiyyah: 'The rare is treated as non-existent.' It explains that things which occur infrequently are given the legal status of non-existence. The author applies this to the ruling on fasting: if a person eats or drinks by mistake (khata') or under coercion (ikrah), his fast breaks and he must perform qada' (make up the day). Imam al-Shafi'i analogized mistake and coercion to forgetfulness (nisyan), which does not break the fast. The Hanafi response is twofold: first, forgetfulness is frequent, while mistake and coercion are rare, so the latter are treated as non-existent by this qaa'idah; second, forgetfulness is from Allah (the One whose right is involved), while mistake and coercion come from human agents, so they differ analogously to the difference between a restrained person and a sick person in prayer qada'.

Key Terms
النادر (al-nādir): That which occurs infrequently, such as eating/drinking by mistake or under coercion.
خطأ (khaṭa'): An unintentional act committed by mistake, without intent.
إكراه (ikrāh): Compulsion by another person to perform an act against one's will.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school rule that a fast is invalidated by mistake or coercion but not by forgetfulness?
A) Because mistake and coercion are intentional acts.
B) Because forgetfulness is rare while mistake and coercion are frequent.
C) Because forgetfulness is frequent and from Allah, while mistake and coercion are rare and from humans.
D) Because the hadith only covers forgetfulness.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi maxim 'the rare is like non-existent' distinguishes mistake and coercion from forgetfulness, making qada' obligatory for the former but not the latter.

The Rare Is Like the Nonexistent: Qada for Mistake and Coercion in Fasting
Inter-madhhab dispute on qada for error/coercion in fasting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school obligates qada for a fasting person who breaks the fast by mistake or coercion, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
The rare is treated as nonexistent: error and coercion are rare, so qada is wajib, unlike frequent forgetfulness.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on qada for a fasting person who mistakenly eats or is coerced into eating. The key is the legal maxim 'al-nadir ka'l-ma'dum'—the rare is like the nonexistent. Understanding this maxim and its application to the dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i will clarify a common point of difference between the schools.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Clearly state the Hanafi ruling: qada is wajib (obligatory) for both the mistaken (mukhti') and the coerced (mukrah) fasting person.
This is the mufta bihi position from Zahir al-Riwayah.
2
Identify the opponent and their proof
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that no qada is required, analogizing error and coercion to forgetfulness (nisyan), which does not break the fast.
The Shafi'i qiyas is based on the common element of lack of intent.
3
Follow the Hanafi response
Hanafis distinguish: forgetfulness is frequent (kathir al-wuqu'), while error and coercion are rare (nadir). The maxim 'al-nadir ka'l-ma'dum' means rare events are legally disregarded, so the analogy fails. Additionally, forgetfulness is from Allah (the rights-holder), while coercion is from others.
A further analogy with prayer (muqayyad vs. sick) is cited to support the distinction.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: Hanafi mufti will require qada; Shafi'i mufti will not.
This affects fatwa in cases of accidental eating/drinking during Ramadan fasts.

Source Text

الهداية: كتاب الصوم، باب ما يوجب القضاء والكفارة، ج ۱ ص ۲۳۴، ط: رحمانيه لِأَنَّهُ لَوْ كَانَ مُخْطِئًا أَوْ مُكْرَهَا فَعَلَيْهِ الْقَضَاءِ حِلَافًا لِلشَّافِعِيِّ، فَإِنَّهُ يَعْتَبِرُ بِالنَّاسِي، وَلَنَا أَنَّهُ لَا يَغْلِبُ وُجُودَهُ وَعُذْرُ النِّسْيَانِ غَالِبٌ، وَلَأَنَّ النِّسْيَانَ مِنْ قِبَلِ مَنْ لَهُ الْحَقُّ، وَالْإِكْرَاهُ مِنْ قِبَلِ غَيْرِهِ فَيَفْتَرِقَانِ كَالْمُقَيَّدِ وَالْمَرِيضِ الْعَاجِزِ عَنْ الْآدَاءِ بِالرَّأْسِ فِي قَضَاءِ الصَّلَاةِ حَيْثُ يَقْضِي الْمُقَيَّدُ لَا الْمَرِيضُ. البحر الرائق: كتاب الصوم، باب ما يفسد الصوم وما لا يفسده، ج ۲ ص ۲۹۲، ط: دار الكتاب الإسلامي]
The rare is like the nonexistent. Translation: The rare is like the nonexistent. Explanation: The meaning of the aforementioned principle is that a thing whose existence is rare is in the ruling of nonexistent, i.e., an infrequent occurrence is in the ruling of nonexistent. The author of Al-Hidayah mentions this principle to prove that qada is obligatory upon the mistaken and the coerced. If a fasting person mistakenly eats or drinks something, or someone forcefully feeds him, then according to us his fast is broken and qada is obligatory upon him. Imam al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him) says: Just as eating and drinking forgetfully does not break the fast, similarly in error and coercion the fast is not broken. The Hanafis say: There is a clear difference between forgetfulness and error/coercion, so analogizing one to the other is not correct. Forgetfulness is frequent in occurrence, while error and coercion are rare in occurrence, and the rare is in the ruling of the nonexistent. Also, forgetfulness is from the side of the One who has the right (i.e., Allah), while error and coercion are from the side of others. Al-Hidayah: ... because if he was mistaken or coerced, then qada is upon him, contrary to al-Shafi'i, for he analogizes to the forgetful person. Our argument is that it (error/coercion) does not occur frequently, and the excuse of forgetfulness is frequent, and because forgetfulness is from the one who has the right, and coercion is from other than him, so they differ, like the fettered (muqayyad) and the sick person unable to perform with the head in making up prayer, where the fettered makes up but the sick does not. Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: ... [end]
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute. The principle 'The rare is like the nonexistent' is invoked to distinguish error and coercion from forgetfulness in the context of breaking the fast. The Hanafis argue that since error and coercion are rare occurrences, they are treated as nonexistent legal constants, thus qada is obligatory. Imam al-Shafi'i analogizes them to forgetfulness, which does not require qada. The Hanafi response includes two arguments: (1) frequency—forgetfulness is common, error/coercion rare; (2) source—forgetfulness is from Allah (the rights-holder), while error/coercion are from humans. An analogy with prayer is given: a fettered person (who cannot stand but can gesture with head) must make up missed prayers, while a sick person (unable to gesture) does not, highlighting that different causes lead to different rulings. Thus, the Hanafi position is that qada is obligatory for both the mistaken and coerced fasting person.

Key Terms
النَّادِر (al-nadir): That which occurs rarely, as opposed to الغالب (al-ghalib, frequent).
مُخْطِئ (mukhti'): One who errs, makes a mistake unintentionally.
مُكْرَه (mukrah): One who is coerced, forced to act against his will.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is qada obligatory for a fasting person who breaks the fast by mistake but not for one who forgets?
A) Because mistake is intentional, while forgetfulness is not.
B) Because mistake is rare, while forgetfulness is frequent, and the rare is treated as nonexistent.
C) Because the Prophet (peace be upon him) explicitly distinguished the two cases.
D) Because the mistake involves eating, while forgetfulness does not.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school obligates qada for breaking the fast by mistake or coercion, based on the maxim that the rare is like the nonexistent, distinguishing these cases from forgetfulness.
