The author of Hidayah, by mentioning this principle, is stating that if a child reaches puberty or a disbeliever accepts Islam during Ramadan, they must refrain from eating and drinking etc. for the remaining part of the day, and they do not have to make up that day, nor do they have to make up previous days. Previous days are not made up because before puberty and before accepting Islam, the address (of obligation) was not directed to them. That day is not made up because, in fasting, the cause of obligation is the initial part of the day, and the initial part of the day is connected to the dawn; at that time, these people lacked eligibility (ahliyyah). Therefore, the fast of that day was not obligatory upon them, and when the fast was not obligatory, its makeup is also not incumbent. Unlike prayer, for which makeup is obligatory because in prayer the cause of obligation is the part that is connected to performance, and in that part, eligibility was found in them. Hence, when at the time of the cause of obligation eligibility was found, (then prayer becomes obligatory upon them; therefore, if they do not pray, makeup is obligatory). (And whoever dies with a makeup of Ramadan due upon him and bequeaths it, food is given on his behalf (by his heir as an obligation if it comes out of one-third of his estate; otherwise, within one-third) for each day half a sa' of wheat or a sa' of dates or a sa' of barley, because he was unable to perform at the end of his life, so he became like a decrepit old man. Then, a will is necessary according to us, such that if he dies and does not bequeath feeding on his behalf, it is not obligatory upon his heirs; but if they voluntarily do so without a will, it is permissible. And similarly, zakat. [Al-Lubab fi Sharh al-Kitab: Kitab al-Sawm, vol. 1, ed. al-Maktabah al-Ilmiyyah] [Al-Hidayah: Kitab al-Sawm, Bab ma yujib al-qada wa al-kaffarah, vol. 1, p. 241, ed. Rahmaniyyah] So prayer becomes obligatory upon them; therefore, if they do not pray, makeup is obligatory.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory commentary on a principle from Al-Hidayah concerning the cause of obligation (sabab al-wujub) in prayer versus fasting. The author first states the principle: in prayer, the cause of obligation is the part that is connected to performance, meaning the time when the person is actually capable of performing the prayer (e.g., the final portion of the prayer time); in fasting, the cause of obligation is the first part of the day, i.e., dawn (fajr). The commentary then applies this to a common scenario: a child who reaches puberty during a day in Ramadan or a non-Muslim who embraces Islam during a day in Ramadan. According to the principle, they must abstain from eating/drinking for the rest of the day out of respect for the time, but they do not need to make up that day nor any previous days of Ramadan. Why? Because for previous days, there was no address of obligation (khitab) before they had the capacity (ahliyyah) – the child was not yet an adult, the non-Muslim was not yet a Muslim. For the current day, the cause of obligation (sabab) is the initial part of the day (dawn), and at dawn they lacked eligibility (e.g., the child was still a minor, the non-Muslim was still a kafir at dawn). Thus, the fast was never obligatory on them for that day, so no makeup is required. In contrast, for prayer, the cause of obligation is the connected part (e.g., the last part of the prayer time). If they become eligible (e.g., reach puberty or accept Islam) before that time ends, the prayer becomes obligatory, and if they miss it, they must make it up. The passage then includes a related ruling from Al-Lubab about a person who dies with outstanding Ramadan fasts and leaves a will for feeding (fidya). According to the Hanafi school, if the will is made and the estate can cover it, feeding is obligatory; otherwise, it is limited to one-third. Without a will, the heirs are not obliged to feed, but if they do so voluntarily, it is valid. This ruling is analogous to zakat. The commentary concludes by emphasizing the difference between prayer and fasting.

Key Terms
سبب الوجوب (sabab al-wujub): The cause or occasion that makes an act obligatory; here it refers to the specific part of time that triggers obligation.
الجزء المتصل بالاداء (al-juz' al-muttasil bi al-ada'): The part of time that is immediately connected to the performance of the act, i.e., the final portion of the prayer time in which one can still pray.
الجزء الأول (al-juz' al-awwal): The first part of the day, i.e., dawn (fajr), which is the cause of obligation for fasting.
أهلية (ahliyyah): Legal capacity/eligibility; for the child, it is bulugh; for the kafir, it is Islam.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A boy reaches puberty at 10 AM during Ramadan. According to the Hanafi school, which of the following is correct?
A) He must fast the rest of the day and make up that day after Ramadan.
B) He must fast the rest of the day but does not need to make up that day.
C) He does not need to fast the rest of the day nor make it up.
D) He must make up all missed days of Ramadan from the beginning.

Key Takeaway
The cause of obligation (sabab al-wujub) for fasting is dawn, while for prayer it is the connected part of the time; eligibility at the cause determines whether qada is required.

Sawm — Fasting

7 min
Makeup of Missed Fasts and Fidya upon Death
Fidya ruling for missed fasts upon death

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on making a will for feeding (fidya) when one dies with outstanding Ramadan fasts, including when it becomes obligatory and when it is permissible.

Core Idea
A will for feeding is obligatory if the estate can cover it; otherwise, limited to one-third.

When a person dies with missed Ramadan fasts, the Hanafi school requires that if they made a will for feeding (fidya), the heirs must carry it out from the estate. This lesson examines the conditions and limits of this ruling, as stated in Al-Lubab and Al-Hidayah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
A person dies with outstanding qada for Ramadan fasts and leaves a will (wasiyyah) stating that feeding (it'am) should be done on their behalf.
The ruling also applies similarly to zakat.
2
Determine the obligation for the heirs
According to the Hanafi school, if the will is made and the estate can cover the feeding from the one-third that is permissible for bequests, then feeding becomes wajib (obligatory) on the heirs to carry out.
If the estate cannot cover it fully, the feeding is limited to the one-third portion.
3
Recognize the exception
If no will was made, the heirs are not obliged to feed, but if they do so voluntarily, it is valid and accepted.
This is based on the principle that the missed fasts are akin to a debt that can be expiated by feeding.
4
Analogous ruling for zakat
The same ruling applies to zakat: if a person dies with unpaid zakat and makes a will, the heirs must pay it from the estate within the one-third limit.
Zakat and fasting are both rights of Allah that become debts upon the deceased.
5
Understand the reason
The person is considered 'ajiz' (incapable) at the end of life, like a decrepit old man who cannot fast, so fidya is given instead.
This is from the Hanafi school; other schools may differ.

Travel and the Validity of Fasting Intention Before Midday
Establishes a principle and applies it to the traveler's intention case

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the validity of a traveler's fasting intention made before midday and apply it to a scenario, including the consequential absence of kaffarah.

Core Idea
Travel does not remove eligibility for obligation; a traveler's fast is valid if intended before midday.

This lesson examines a key principle in Hanafi fiqh regarding fasting and travel. The text establishes that travel does not negate the eligibility for obligation (ahliyyat al-wujub) nor the validity of commencement (sihhat al-shuru'). It applies this to a specific case: a traveler who initially intended to break fast but later returns home and makes a new intention before midday. According to the Hanafi school, this fast is valid. However, if he actually breaks it, no expiation (kaffarah) is required due to a doubt of permissibility (shubhat al-mubih).

Guided Path
1
Identify the Principle
The passage states a foundational rule: travel does not affect ahliyyat al-wujub (legal capacity to be obligated) nor sihhat al-shuru' (validity of commencing the fast). This means a traveler retains full capacity and can validly start a fast even after initially intending to break it.
This principle is general and applies to all acts of worship, not just fasting.
2
Understand the Scenario
Consider a traveler who, upon waking, intends to break his fast due to travel. Later, he returns home before midday and makes a new intention to fast. The question is whether this fast is valid.
The key turning point is the return to residence before midday.
3
Apply the Principle
Since travel does not negate capacity or validity of commencement, the traveler's new intention is effective. The fast is valid, provided the intention is made before midday (i.e., before the end of the time when it is normally permissible to begin a fast).
The condition 'before midday' is inferred from general Hanafi rulings on niyyah for obligatory fasts.
4
Recognise the Exception Regarding Kaffarah
If the traveler actually breaks this valid fast (e.g., by eating after intending), no kaffarah is required. This is because at the time of breaking, there remains a doubt (shubhah) of permissibility due to his initial travel and the fact that he had earlier intended to break. Such a doubt prevents the imposition of expiation.
Kaffarah is only due when there is a clear violation without any plausible excuse.
5
Compare with an Analogous Case
The text draws an analogy: if a resident at the beginning of the day later travels, he is not allowed to break his fast because the aspect of residence takes precedence. Similarly, when the traveler becomes resident at the time of intention, the obligation to fast becomes binding, even more so.
This analogy (qiyas) reinforces the ruling and helps understand the underlying rationale.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إِنَّ السَّفَرَ لَا يُنَافِي أَهْلِيَّةَ الْوُجُوبِ وَلَا صِحَّةَ الشُّرُوعِ. وَلَوْ نَوَى الْمُسَافِرُ الْإِفْطَارَ ثُمَّ قَدِمَ وَنَوَى الصَّوْمَ فِي وَقْتِهِ صَحَّ إِنْ نَوَى قَبْلَ انْتِصَافِ النَّهَارِ، لِأَنَّ السَّفَرَ لَا يُنَافِي أَهْلِيَّةَ الْوُجُوبِ وَلَا صِحَّةَ الشُّرُوعِ، أَطْلَقَ الصَّوْمَ فَشَمِلَ الْفَرْضَ الَّذِي لَا يُشْتَرَطُ فِيهِ التَّبْيِيتُ وَالنَّفَلَ. وَحَيْثُ أَفَادَ صِحَّةَ صَوْمِ الْفَرْضِ لَزِمَ عَلَيْهِ صَوْمُهُ إِنْ كَانَ فِي رَمَضَانَ لِزَوَالِ الْمُرَخَّصِ فِي وَقْتِ النِّيَّةِ. أَلَا تَرَى أَنَّهُ لَوْ كَانَ مُقِيمًا فِي أَوَّلِ الْيَوْمِ ثُمَّ سَافَرَ لَا يُبَاحُ لَهُ الْفِطْرُ تَرْجِيحًا لِجَانِبِ الْإِقَامَةِ فَهَذَا أَوْلَى. إِلَّا أَنَّهُ إِذَا أَفْطَرَ فِي الْمَسْأَلَتَيْنِ لَا كَفَّارَةَ عَلَيْهِ لِقِيَامِ شُبْهَةِ الْمُبِيحِ.
Travel does not negate the eligibility for obligation nor the validity of commencement. And if a traveler intended break-fast, then he arrived (home) and intended fasting in its time, it is valid if he intended before midday, because travel does not negate eligibility for obligation nor validity of commencement. He used the absolute term 'fasting' thus it includes obligatory fasts for which pre-dawn intention is not required and supererogatory fasts. And since it yielded the validity of obligatory fasting, it is necessary upon him to fast if it is in Ramadan because the concession is removed at the time of intention. Do you not see that if he was resident at the beginning of the day then traveled, breaking fast is not permitted for him, giving precedence to the aspect of residence, so this is more so. Except that if he broke fast in either of the two cases, there is no expiation upon him due to the presence of a doubt of the permissibility [of breaking fast].
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This is a ruling with proof regarding the effect of travel on fasting. The principle states that travel does not negate two things: (1) the eligibility for obligation (ahliyyat al-wujub), meaning the capacity to be obligated, and (2) the validity of commencement (sihhat al-shuru'), meaning the act of starting the fast. The text then gives a specific case: a traveler who intended to break his fast (i.e., decided not to fast that day), but then returned to his place of residence before midday and made a new intention to fast. According to Hanafi law, his fast is valid, provided he intended before midday. This is because travel does not remove his eligibility for the obligation nor affect the validity of starting the fast. The text elaborates that the word 'fasting' here is general, covering both obligatory fasts that do not require pre-dawn intention (like Ramadan for a resident) and supererogatory fasts. Since this ruling confirms the validity of an obligatory fast in Ramadan, fasting becomes binding upon him because the concession (rukhsah) to break fast due to travel is no longer present at the time of intention. The text draws an analogy: if a resident at the beginning of the day later travels, he is not allowed to break fast because the aspect of residence has precedence. Here, the traveler becomes resident at the time of intention, so it is even more fitting that he must fast. However, if he actually breaks the fast, he does not incur expiation (kaffarah) because there is a doubt (shubhah) of permissibility due to his initial travel.

Key Terms
أهلية الوجوب (ahliyyat al-wujub): The legal capacity to be obligated to perform an act of worship.
صحة الشروع (sihhat al-shuru'): The validity of commencing the act of worship after making intention.
شبهة المباح (shubhat al-mubih): A doubt regarding permissibility that prevents the imposition of expiation (kaffarah).
التَّبْييت (al-tabyit): The intention made during the night before dawn for a fast.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A traveler intends to break his fast in the morning, then returns home at 10 AM and intends to fast. According to the Hanafi ruling:
A) His fast is invalid regardless.
B) His fast is valid if he intended before midday.
C) His fast is valid only if he intended before dawn.
D) His fast is valid but he must make it up later.

Key Takeaway
The fast of a traveler is valid if intended before midday, and no kaffarah is due if broken due to the doubt of permissibility (shubhat al-mubih) arising from the initial travel.

Ruling When an Excuse Ends During the Day
Lists exempted persons and rules on abstaining and qada

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for exempted individuals who become capable mid-day, identify the reason why qada is not required, and distinguish it from the ruling for prayer.

Core Idea
Excuse ends mid-day? Abstain but no qada—the obligation trigger was absent.

This passage from al-Hidayah addresses a common scenario: what happens when a person's excuse for not fasting ends during a day of Ramadan? The text lists several cases (boy reaching puberty, non-Muslim embracing Islam, menstruating woman, etc.) and establishes a uniform ruling. Understanding this ruling requires grasping the Hanafi distinction between the cause of obligation (sabab) for fasting versus prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the individuals and their shared ruling
List all categories mentioned (child reaching puberty, non-Muslim converting, menstruating woman, etc.) and note that they all must abstain from breakers for the rest of the day but are not required to make up that day.
The text groups them under 'every excused person whose excuse ceases after dawn'.
2
Understand the basis for abstaining without qada
The text gives two reasons: 'fulfilling the right of the time by imitation' and 'so that he does not expose himself to suspicion'. This makes abstaining wajib out of respect, not due to the original obligation.
This is a key Hanafi principle: respect for the sacred time.
3
Analyze the distinction from prayer
The passage contrasts this with prayer: if eligibility (puberty/Islam) occurs during a prayer time, that prayer must be made up. The difference lies in the nature of the cause (sabab). For fasting, the cause is the first part of the day; for prayer, it is the part connected to performance.
This is a qiyas khafi (subtle analogy) showing how the sabab determines the obligation.
4
Apply the condition: excuse ending before dawn
If the excuse ends before dawn, then fasting is obligatory for the entire day and qada is not an issue. This is the default rule; the passage's scenario is the exception.
The text states: 'As for if it ceases before the break of dawn, then fasting is incumbent upon him.'

وَإِذَا بَلَغَ الصَّبِيُّ أَوْ أَسْلَمَ الْكَافِرُ فِي رَمَضَانَ أَيْ فِي يَوْمٍ مِنْ أَيَّامِ رَمَضَانَ أَمْسَكَا بَقِيَّةَ يَوْمِهِمَا، وَكَذَلِكَ الْحَائِضُ إِذَا طَهُرَتْ وَالنُّفَسَاءُ وَالْمَجْنُونُ إِذَا أَفَاقَ وَالْمَرِيضُ إِذَا بَرِئَ وَالْمُسَافِرُ إِذَا أَقَامَ، فَحُكْمُ هَؤُلَاءِ فِي الْإِمْسَاكِ عَنِ الْمُفْطِرَاتِ سَوَاءٌ، وَهَكَذَا كُلُّ مَعْذُورٍ زَالَ عُذْرُهُ بَعْدَ طُلُوعِ الْفَجْرِ. أَمَّا لَوْ زَالَ قَبْلَ طُلُوعِ الْفَجْرِ لَزِمَهُ الصَّوْمُ... قَضَاءً لِحَقِّ الْوَقْتِ بِالتَّشَبُّهِ، يَعْنِي لِقَضَاءِ حَقِّ الْوَقْتِ بِالتَّشَبُّهِ بِالصَّائِمِينَ، وَلِئَلَّا يُعَرِّضَ نَفْسَهُ لِلتُّهْمَةِ... وَصَامَا مَا بَعْدَهُ لِتَحَقُّقِ السَّبَبِ وَهُوَ شُهُودُ الشَّهْرِ وَالْأَهْلِيَةِ الْإِسْلَامِ وَالْبُلُوغِ. وَلَمْ يَقْضِيَا يَوْمَهُمَا الَّذِي بَلَغَ فِيهِ الصَّبِيُّ وَأَسْلَمَ الْكَافِرُ وَلَا مَا مَضَى مِنَ الْأَيَّامِ لِعَدَمِ الْخِطَابِ، لِأَنَّ الْخِطَابَ إِنَّمَا يَكُونُ عِنْدَ الْأَهْلِيَّةِ وَكَانَتْ مُنْتَفِيَةً. وَهَذَا بِخِلَافِ الصَّلَاةِ، أَيْ هَذَا الْحُكْمُ الَّذِي ذَكَرْنَاهُ وَهُوَ عَدَمُ وُجُوبِ قَضَاءِ صَوْمِ ذَلِكَ الْيَوْمِ الَّذِي بَلَغَ فِيهِ الصَّبِيُّ أَوْ أَسْلَمَ الْكَافِرُ، بِخِلَافِ الصَّلَاةِ يَجِبُ قَضَاؤُهَا إِذَا بَلَغَ أَوْ أَسْلَمَ فِي بَعْضِ الْوَقْتِ، لِأَنَّ السَّبَبَ فِي وُجُوبِ الصَّلَاةِ الْجُزْءُ الْمُتَّصِلُ بِالْأَدَاءِ، فَوُجِدَتِ الْأَهْلِيَّةُ عِنْدَهُ، وَفِي الصَّوْمِ الْجُزْءُ الْأَوَّلُ، وَالْأَهْلِيَّةُ مُنْعَدِمَةٌ عِنْدَ الْجُزْءِ الْأَوَّلِ.
And when a boy reaches puberty or an infidel embraces Islam during Ramadan, i.e., during a day of the days of Ramadan, they abstain for the remainder of their day; and likewise the menstruating woman when she becomes pure, and the woman in postpartum bleeding, and the insane person when he recovers sanity, and the sick person when he recovers, and a traveler when he takes up residence. So the ruling of these persons regarding abstaining from things that break fast is the same. And likewise every excused person whose excuse ceases after the break of dawn. As for if it ceases before the break of dawn, then fasting is incumbent upon him... fulfilling the right of the time by imitation, meaning to fulfill the right of the time by imitating those who fast, and so that he does not expose himself to suspicion... And they fast what comes after due to the actualization of the cause which is witnessing the month, and eligibility, Islam, and puberty. And they do not make up their day in which the boy reached puberty and the infidel embraced Islam, nor the days that have passed, due to the absence of the address [of obligation]. Because the address is only at the time of eligibility and it was absent. And this is different from prayer, i.e., this ruling we mentioned, which is the non-obligation of making up the fast of that day in which the boy reached puberty or the infidel embraced Islam, unlike prayer which must be made up if he reaches puberty or embraces Islam during part of the time, because the cause in the obligation of prayer is the part connected to performance, so eligibility is found at that time. And in fasting, [the cause is] the first part, and eligibility is absent at the first part.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This is a ruling with proof regarding various individuals whose excuse for missing a fast ends during the day. The text lists: a boy reaching puberty, a non-Muslim embracing Islam, a menstruating woman becoming pure, a woman in postpartum bleeding becoming clean, an insane person regaining sanity, a sick person recovering, and a traveler taking up residence. All these must abstain from things that break fast for the remainder of that day, even though they are not obligated to make up that day's fast. The reason given is that they must show respect for the time by imitating those who are fasting, and to avoid suspicion. If their excuse ends before dawn, then they must fast. However, they are not required to make up that day because the address of obligation (khitab) was absent at the relevant part of the day. For fasting, the cause of obligation is the first part of the day (the part connected to the fast), and eligibility was absent then. This differs from prayer: if a boy reaches puberty or a non-Muslim converts during the time for a prayer, he must make up that prayer because the cause of obligation in prayer is the part of the time connected to actual performance, and eligibility exists at that moment. So the comparison clarifies that the ruling for fasting is based on the specific nature of its obligation.

Key Terms
معذور (ma'dhur): A person who has a valid excuse (like travel, illness, menstruation, etc.) that exempts them from fasting.
خطاب (khitab): The divine address (command) that imposes obligation.
سبب (sabab): The cause or occasion that triggers the obligation of an act of worship.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A boy reaches puberty at 10 AM during Ramadan. According to the Hanafi ruling:
A) He must fast the rest of the day and also make up that day later.
B) He must fast the rest of the day but does not make up that day.
C) He is not required to fast that day at all.
D) He must make up the entire month.

Key Takeaway
When an excused person becomes eligible mid-day, abstaining is wajib but qada is not required because the cause of obligation was absent at its moment.

Separate Intention Required for Each Day of Fasting
States principle of renewing intention each day

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule that intention must be renewed for each day of fasting and apply it to scenarios where intention may be interrupted.

Core Idea
Intention must be renewed each day — the fast of each day is an independent act of worship.

This lesson establishes a fundamental principle in the fiqh of fasting: each day of Ramadan requires its own separate intention (niyyah). Many assume that a single intention at the start of the month suffices, but the Hanafi school emphasizes that each day is an independent act of worship. Understanding this rule is crucial for the validity of one's fasts and for correctly handling cases where intention may be absent due to unconsciousness, forgetfulness, or ignorance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is fasting (sawm) in Ramadan. The ruling stated is that intention (niyyah) is required for each day separately. This is a wajib ruling—failure to renew intention invalidates the fast.
Intention is a condition (shart) for validity, not just a recommended act.
2
Understand the reason for separate intention
Each day of fasting is an independent act of worship because the night separates the days. The intention for one day does not carry over to the next.
This contrasts with acts like wudu, where one intention suffices for multiple prayers.
3
Apply the rule to the unconscious person scenario
Consider a person who becomes unconscious before the new moon is sighted and remains unconscious for the entire month. Because no valid intention was made for any day (intention before moon sighting is not valid), he must make up all days except the first day (if he became unconscious after sighting, the first day is counted as intended).
This illustrates the importance of the timing and existence of intention.
4
Contrast with other types of fasts
For voluntary (nafl) fasts, the intention can be made until midday. For obligatory fasts (Ramadan, qada, expiation), intention must be made before dawn. This distinction highlights the stricter requirement for obligatory acts.
The ruling in this lesson applies specifically to obligatory fasts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لَا بُدَّ مِنْ نِيَّةِ الصَّوْمِ لِكُلِّ يَوْمٍ عَلَى حِدَةٍ.
It is necessary to have intention of fasting for each day separately.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This is a ruling statement establishing the general principle that for fasting, a separate intention is required for each day. The reason is that each day of fasting is an independent act of worship, separated by the night which is not a time for fasting. The text then applies this: if a person becomes unconscious from the first night of Ramadan before the new moon is sighted and remains unconscious the entire month, he must make up all days except the first. The first day is presumed to have been intended because a Muslim normally intends the first day upon sighting the moon. But if he became unconscious before the moon was sighted, even the first day must be made up because intention before sighting is not valid. This shows the importance of renewing intention each day.

Key Terms
نية (niyyah): The intention to perform an act of worship, which differentiates it from custom.
قضاء (qada'): Making up a missed obligatory act of worship after its prescribed time.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on intention for each day of Ramadan fasting according to the Hanafi school?
A) Sunnah
B) Wajib
C) Mustahab
D) Mubah

Key Takeaway
Each day of fasting is a separate act of worship requiring its own intention.

Sawm — Fasting
intermediate
12 min
Separate Intention for Each Day of Fasting
States the principle of separate intention per day and its application to unconsciousness and traveler cases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that each day of fasting requires its own intention, explain the rational proof, and apply it to the case of unconsciousness.

Core Idea
Each day's fast is a separate worship; night interrupts, so intention must be renewed daily.

In the Hanafi school, the intention for fasting is not a single act covering the entire month. Rather, each day of Ramadan is an independent act of worship, requiring its own intention. This principle is derived from the fact that the night between two days is not a time for fasting, breaking the continuity. The classical text of al-Hidayah and its commentary apply this rule to a case of prolonged unconsciousness, illustrating its practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
State the Ruling
Identify the core Hanafi ruling: it is necessary (wajib) to make a separate intention for each day of fasting, whether in Ramadan or for other obligatory fasts.
This is the mufta bihi position.
2
Trace the Proof
Understand the rational proof (dalil aqli): fasting is tied to the day (daylight hours). Each day is separated by a night, which is not a time for fasting. Therefore, each day's fast is a distinct act, requiring its own intention.
Contrast: Imam Malik considers the entire month as one continuous act.
3
Apply to Unconsciousness Scenario
If a person becomes unconscious from the first night of Ramadan and remains unconscious all month, the first day's fast is valid because we presume he intended it at moon sighting. The remaining days require qada because intention was absent. If unconsciousness began before sighting, even the first day requires qada.
The presumption of intention for the first day is based on the normal practice of Muslims.
4
Consider the Madhhab Dispute
Imam Malik holds that one intention at the beginning of Ramadan suffices for the entire month. The Hanafi response is that each day is separate due to the night break.
Thamarah al-khilaf: Hanafis must renew intention each night; Malikis need not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(١) لَا بُدَّ مِنْ نِيَّةِ الصَّوْمِ لِكُلِّ يَوْمٍ عَلَى حِدَةٍ.
ترجمہ: روزے کے ہر دن کے لئے علیحدہ طور پر نیت کرنا ضروری ہے۔
تشریح: مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ روزے میں ہر دن کے لئے الگ نیت کرنا ضروری ہے، کیونکہ روزے کا تعلق دن سے ہے جب کہ ہر دن کے بعد رات کی شکل میں ایک ایسا زمانہ آتا ہے جس میں نیت نہیں کی جاسکتی اور نہ اس میں روزہ رکھا جا سکتا ہے، اس لئے ہر روزے کے لئے الگ الگ نیت ضروری ہے۔ صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلارہے ہیں کہ اگر کسی آدمی پر رمضان کی پہلی رات سے بے ہوشی طاری ہو گئی اور پورا ماہ بے ہوش رہا ، تو پہلے روزے کے علاوہ پورے ماہ کی قضاء واجب ہے، کیونکہ رمضان کا چاند نظر آنے کے بعد مسلمان کا ظاہر حال یہی ہے کہ اس نے پہلے روزے کی نیت کر لی ہوگی ، اور جب پہلے روزے کی نیت کر لی تو اس کا یہ روزہ شرعا معتبر ہوگا اور اس کی قضاء واجب نہ ہوگی۔ چونکہ اس کے بعد کے روزوں کی نیت نہیں پائی گئی اس لئے ان کی قضاء واجب ہوگی ۔ ہاں اگر یہ شخص چاند نظر آنے سے پہلے ہی بے ہوش ہو گیا تو پہلے روزے کی بھی قضاء واجب ہوگی، کیونکہ چاند نظر آنے سے پہلے کی نیت معتبر نہیں ہے۔ امام مالک رحمہ اللہ کے (قَوْلُهُ : وَلَوْ نَوَى الْمُسَافِرُ الْإِفْطَارَ ثُمَّ قَدِمَ وَنَوَى الصَّوْمَ فِى وَقْتِهِ صَحٌ) إِنْ نَوَى قَبْلَ انْتِصَافِ النَّهَارِ، لِأَنَّ السَّفَرَ لَا يُنَا فِي أَهْلِيَّةَ الْوُجُوبِ وَلَا صِحَّةَ الشُّرُوعِ أَطْلَقَ الصَّوْمَ فَشَمِلَ الْفَرْضَ الَّذِي لَا يُشْتَرَطُ فِيهِ التَّبييتُ وَالنَّفَلَ وَحَيْتُ أَفَادَ صِحَّةَ صَوْمِ الْفَرْضِ لَزِمَ عَلَيْهِ صَوْمُهُ إِنْ كَانَ فِي رَمَضَانَ لِزَوَالِ الْمُرَخَّصِ فِى وَقْتِ النِّيَّةِ أَلَا تَرَى أَنَّهُ لَوْ كَانَ مُقِيمًا فِي أَوَّلِ الْيَوْمِ ثُمَّ سَافَرَ لَا يُبَاحُ لَهُ الْفِطْرُ تَرْجِيحًا لِجَانِبِ الْإِقَامَةِ فَهَذَا أَوْلَى إِلَّا أَنَّهُ إِذَا أَفْطَرَ فِي الْمَسْأَلَتَيْن لَا كَفَّارَةَ عَلَيْهِ لِقِيَامِ شُبُهَةِ الْمُبِيحِ.
البحر الرائق: كتاب الصوم، باب ما يفسد الصوم وما لا يفسده، ج ۲ ص ۲۹۸، ط: دار الكتاب الإسلامي]
الهداية كتاب الصوم، باب ما يوجب القضاء والكفارة، ج ۱ ص ۲۴۲، ط: رحمانيه
It is necessary to have intention for fasting for each day separately. Translation: It is essential to make intention for each day of fasting separately. Explanation: The meaning of this principle is that in fasting, it is necessary to make a separate intention for each day because fasting is related to the day, and after each day comes a period of night during which intention cannot be made nor fasting observed. Therefore, a separate intention is necessary for each fast. The author of the Hidayah, by mentioning this principle, is explaining that if a person becomes unconscious from the first night of Ramadan and remains unconscious for the whole month, then qada is wajib for the entire month except the first fast, because after sighting the crescent, the apparent state of a Muslim is that he would have intended for the first fast. And when he intended for the first fast, his fast is legally valid and qada is not wajib. Since intention for the subsequent fasts was not found, their qada is wajib. Yes, if this person became unconscious before sighting the crescent, then qada is also wajib for the first fast because intention before sighting is not valid. On the saying of Imam Malik (may Allah have mercy on him): 'And if a traveler intends to break the fast then returns and intends fasting in its time, it is valid if he intends before midday, because travel does not negate the capacity for obligation nor the validity of commencement. He used the absolute term fasting, so it includes obligatory fast for which night intention is not required, and supererogatory fast. And since it establishes the validity of obligatory fast, he must observe it if it is in Ramadan due to the removal of concession at the time of intention. Do you not see that if he was resident at the beginning of the day then traveled, breaking fast is not permissible for him, giving preference to the side of residence, so this is more appropriate. Except that if he breaks fast in either case, there is no kaffarah upon him due to the presence of a doubt of permissibility.' Al-Bahr al-Raiq: Book of Fasting, Chapter on What Invalidates Fasting and What Does Not, vol. 2, p. 298, Dar al-Kitab al-Islami. Al-Hidayah: Book of Fasting, Chapter on What Obligates Qada and Kaffarah, vol. 1, p. 242, Rahmaniyah.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on principle (1) from Usool e Hidayah. It states the Hanafi ruling that each day of fasting requires its own intention, contrary to Imam Malik who permits one intention for the entire month. The proof is that each day is separated by night, which is not a time for fasting, so each day's fast is a separate act of worship. The commentary then applies this to a case of unconsciousness: if a person becomes unconscious from the first night of Ramadan and remains so the whole month, the first fast is valid because we presume he intended it at the time of sighting, but subsequent fasts require qada. If unconsciousness began before sighting, even the first day's fast is not valid and requires qada. The passage then quotes a lengthy passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq regarding a traveler who intends to break fast then returns and intends before midday. It discusses the validity of such a fast, concluding that if he breaks fast, no kaffarah is due because of doubt.

Key Terms
نية (niyyah): Intention, the mental resolve to perform the act of worship for the sake of Allah.
صوم (sawm): Fasting, refraining from food, drink, and marital relations from dawn to sunset.
قضاء (qada'): Making up a missed obligatory fast after Ramadan.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi school, why must a person who was unconscious for the entire month of Ramadan make qada for all days except possibly the first?
A) Because unconsciousness invalidates all worship, but the first day is excused due to the sighting.
B) Because the first day's intention is presumed valid as the person was conscious at moon sighting, but subsequent days require their own intention.
C) Because unconsciousness negates intellect, making all fasts invalid.
D) Because the person can combine all intentions in one day.

Key Takeaway
Each day of fasting is a separate act of worship; intention must be renewed daily, and unconsciousness does not retroactively invalidate a day that was properly intended.

Sawm — Fasting

Intention for Each Day of Ramadan
States the general principle that intention is required for worship, applied to fasting.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school requires a separate intention for each day of Ramadan fasting and distinguish this from the Maliki position.

Core Idea
Each day of Ramadan is a separate act of worship requiring its own intention.

This passage from Al-Hidayah applies the universal principle that intention is necessary for all worship to the specific case of Ramadan fasting. It explains the Hanafi ruling that a new intention must be made for each day because each day’s fast is an independent act of worship, separated by nights that are not part of the fasting time.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is fasting in Ramadan; the ruling is that intention is required for each day.
The Hanafi school considers intention a condition (shart) for validity, and it is wajib to renew it daily.
2
Locate the dalil
The primary dalil is the hadith 'لا عبادة إلا بالنية' (No worship without intention).
This hadith establishes intention as a prerequisite for all acts of worship.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The negation ('لا عبادة') indicates that intention is a condition; without it, the worship is invalid. The Hanafi apply this to each day separately.
The argument uses the fact that each day's fast is an independent act, like separate prayers.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Imam Malik holds that one intention at the beginning of Ramadan suffices for the entire month.
The Maliki view considers the month as a single continuous act of worship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۱۷۹) لَا عِبَادَةَ إِلَّا بِالنِّيَّةِ.
وعندنا لا بد من النية لكل يوم، لأنها أى لأن صيام الشهر (عبادات متفرقة) أى صوم كل يوم عبادة وحدها، ألا ترى أن الفساد في الأصل لا يمنع صحة الباقي، فكانت كصلاة مختلفة فيستدعى لكل نية واحدة لأنه يتخلل بين كل يومين ما ليس بزمان هذه العبادة) وهو الليالي فيبقى صوم كل يوم عبادة طول الشهر فيحتاج إلى تعداد النية بتعداد الأيام ولا عبادة إلا بالنية.
[البناية: كتاب الصوم، باب ما يوجب القضاء والكفارة ، فصل، ج ۴، ص ۹۵ ، دار الكتب العلمية ] الهداية: كتاب الصوم، باب ما يوجب القضاء والكفارة، ج ۱ ص ۲۴۲، ط: رحمانيه
There is no worship except with intention. And according to us, intention is necessary for each day because fasting the month (is separate acts of worship) i.e., each day's fast is an act of worship on its own. Do you not see that corruption in the original does not prevent the validity of the remaining? So it is like different prayers. Each requires its own intention because between each two days there is what is not the time of this worship, namely the nights. So the fast of each day remains an act of worship throughout the month, so it needs multiple intentions equal to the number of days. And there is no worship except with intention. [Al-Binayah: Book of Fasting, Chapter on What Obligates Qada and Kaffarah, Section, vol. 4, p. 95, Dar al-Kutub al-Ilmiyyah] Al-Hidayah: Book of Fasting, Chapter on What Obligates Qada and Kaffarah, vol. 1, p. 242, Rahmaniyah.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on principle (179). It begins with the universal principle 'لا عبادة إلا بالنية' (No worship without intention). It then applies this specifically to Ramadan fasting, stating the Hanafi position that each day requires its own intention. The reason given is that the fasts of the month are separate acts of worship (عبادات متفرقة), unlike the salah which can be combined under one intention. The night between each day is not a time for fasting, so each day's fast is a complete act. The reference to Al-Binayah and Al-Hidayah confirms this ruling.

Key Terms
نية (niyyah): Intention, the prerequisite for valid worship.
عبادة (ibadah): An act of worship.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Each day's fast requires its own intention because each day is a separate act of worship.
C) Intention is not required for fasting if one is determined to fast.
D) Only the first day requires intention.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires a separate intention for each day of Ramadan because each day is a separate act of worship.

Unconsciousness and the Obligation of Qadā' in Fasting
States a principle about unconsciousness affecting fasting obligation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on qadā' for a person unconscious for the entire month of Ramadan and explain the underlying legal principle distinguishing ighmā' from junūn.

Core Idea
Unconsciousness weakens the body but leaves intellect intact, so qadā' remains wajib.

One of the nuanced rulings in the Hanafi school concerns a person who remains unconscious for the entire month of Ramadan. Does such a person owe make-up fasts? The classical Hanafi authorities answer with a clear principle: unconsciousness (ighmā') is like illness—it excuses delay but does not waive the obligation entirely. This lesson unpacks the logical and analogical basis of that ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act under discussion? A person unconscious for the entire month of Ramadan. What is the ruling? The school holds that qadā' (make-up fasts) is wajib.
The act is the missed fasts due to unconsciousness; the ruling is the obligation to compensate them.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites no explicit Quran or hadith but uses qiyās (analogy). The basis is that unconsciousness is a type of illness (marad). In Hanafi fiqh, illness permits breaking the fast temporarily but does not cancel qadā'.
The proof is analogical, not textual primary.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalālah
The key point: unconsciousness weakens the body (quwwa) but does not remove intellect (ḥijā). Since legal responsibility (taklīf) depends on intellect, the obligation to make up the fast remains. This is the 'illa linking to illness.
Compare junūn (madness) which removes intellect and can waive qadā' if covering the entire month.
4
Note the cross-madhhab dimension
Imam Mālik holds that one intention suffices for all Ramadan fasts, implying that unconsciousness might not require separate niyyah. The Hanafi principle here serves as a rebuttal.
The focus remains on the Hanafi position; the Mālikī view is mentioned to highlight the dispute.
5
Evaluate the practical application
If a person is unconscious for any part of Ramadan, qadā' is wajib for those days. Even if unconsciousness covers the entire month, the obligation is not waived. This contrasts with junūn covering the entire month, which does waive qadā'.
For partial unconsciousness, qadā' is also wajib; the same principle applies.

Source Text

ترجمہ: بے ہوشی صرف اعضا کو کمزور کرتی ہے لیکن عقل کو زائل نہیں کرتی ۔ تشریح: مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ بے ہوشی صرف انسان کے اعضاء میں جو قوت اور قرار ہوتا ہے اس کو کمزور کرتی ہے اور عقل کو ختم نہیں کرتی ، بخلاف جنون کے کہ وہ عقل کو زائل کر دیتا ہے۔ صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول کو ذکر کر کے اس بات کو ثابت کر رہے ہیں کہ جس آدمی پر پورے رمضان کے مہینہ میں بے ہوشی طاری رہی تو بعد میں قضاء کرے گا، کیونکہ بے ہوشی بھی بیماری کی ایک قسم ہے اور یہ صرف اعضاء کی قوت کو کمزور کر دیتی ہے عقل پر اثر انداز نہیں ہوتی تو یہ اعماء تاخیر میں تو عذر بن سکتا ہے کہ فی الحال روزے نہ رکھے لیکن بالکل ساقط کرنے میں عذر نہیں بن سکتا، کیونکہ ساقط کرنے میں عذر وہ چیز بن سکتی ہے جو عقل پر اثر انداز ہو جیسے جنون ۔
الإغماء إذا استوعب الشهر لا يسقط القضاء ، لأنه نوع مرض يضعف القوى ولا يزيل الحجا، وكذلك الجنون غير المستوعب لا يسقط القضاء ، لعدم الحرج.
منحة السلوك في شرح تحفة الملوك كتاب الصوم، فصل، ج ۱ ص ۲۷۵، ط: وزارة الأوقاف ] الهداية كتاب الصوم، باب ما يوجب القضاء والكفارة، فصل، ج ۱ ص ۲۴۲
All fasts of Ramadan can be performed with one intention, so there is no need for a separate intention for each fast. The mentioned principle is evidence against Imam Malik (may Allah have mercy on him). (178) Verily, unconsciousness only weakens the strong and does not remove intellect. Translation: Unconsciousness only weakens the limbs but does not remove intellect. Explanation: The meaning of the mentioned principle is that unconsciousness only weakens the strength and stability in a person's limbs and does not destroy intellect, unlike madness which destroys intellect. The author of al-Hidayah, by mentioning this principle, proves that a person who remains unconscious for the entire month of Ramadan will make up the fasts later, because unconsciousness is also a type of illness and it only weakens the strength of the limbs and does not affect intellect. So it can be an excuse for delay (temporarily not fasting) but cannot be an excuse for complete cancellation, because the excuse for cancellation is something that affects intellect, like madness. If unconsciousness covers the entire month, qada is not waived, because it is a type of illness that weakens strength and does not remove intellect. Likewise, madness that does not cover the entire month does not waive qada, due to absence of hardship. Minhat al-Suluk fi Sharh Tuhfat al-Muluk, Book of Fasting, Section, Vol. 1, p. 275, Ministry of Awqaf. Al-Hidayah, Book of Fasting, Chapter on what requires qada and kaffarah, Section, Vol. 1, p. 242.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage presents a legal principle (qāʿida) from al-Hidayah regarding unconsciousness (ighmāʾ) and its effect on the obligation to make up missed fasts. The principle states that unconsciousness weakens the body's strength but does not eliminate intellect (ḥijā). The author uses this to distinguish unconsciousness from madness (junūn), which does remove intellect. The ruling: if someone is unconscious for the entire month of Ramadan, qadā' (make-up fasts) is still obligatory because unconsciousness is like an illness that excuses delay but not total cancellation. The proof is analogical: illness permits breaking the fast temporarily but does not waive qadā'; similarly, unconsciousness does not waive it. The passage also refutes Imam Mālik's view that one intention suffices for all Ramadan fasts, but that is a secondary point. The main focus is on the ruling that unconsciousness over the whole month still requires qadā'.

Key Terms
إغماء (ighmā'): Unconsciousness; a state that weakens bodily strength but does not remove intellect.
قضاء (qadā'): Making up missed obligatory fasts after Ramadan.
حجى (ḥijā): Intellect or mental faculty that distinguishes humans and is the basis of legal responsibility.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because unconsciousness is a type of illness that weakens strength but does not remove intellect
C) Because the Prophet ﷺ specifically exempted the unconscious
D) Because the Hanafi school follows Imam Malik's view

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that unconsciousness, like illness, does not remove the obligation to make up missed fasts because it leaves the intellect intact.

No Worship Without Intention
Principle statement with proof and application to fasting.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that intention is necessary for each day's fast and apply the principle 'no worship without intention' to determine when qadā' is required.

Core Idea
Intention transforms physical abstention into worship; without it, fasting is invalid.

This passage from Al-Hidayah establishes a foundational rule: worship is not valid without intention (niyya). The author applies this rule to the fast of Ramadan, explaining that if a person merely refrains from food and drink without intending to fast, his abstention is not considered worship and he must make up those days. We will examine the proof from the hadith 'Actions are by intentions' and the reasoning that each day's fast is a separate act requiring its own intention.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Principle and Its Proof
Read the opening statement 'لا عبادة إلا بالنية' and the hadith cited. Understand that the principle is universal: every act of worship requires intention.
The hadith is mutawatir in meaning but technically ahad; its thubut is qat'ī due to widespread acceptance.
2
Understand the Application to Fasting
The text argues that each day's fast in Ramadan is an independent worship, because nights separate them and invalidation of one day does not affect the next. Therefore, each day requires its own intention.
This contrasts with a continuous single act like prayer, which requires one intention for the whole.
3
Analyze the Hypothetical Case
Consider a person who stays awake during Ramadan, refrains from eating, drinking, and intercourse, but does not intend to fast. According to the principle, his abstention (imsāk) is not worship because intention is missing.
The act of imsāk is physically the same, but the legal ruling changes based on intention.
4
Determine the Consequence
Since the fast is invalid, the person must perform qadā' (make up those days) after Ramadan. No kaffārah is required because there was no intentional nullification.
Qadā' is wajib in this case; kaffārah applies only when a fast is intentionally broken without excuse.
5
Contrast with Other Schools
Imam Malik holds that one intention at the beginning of Ramadan suffices for the entire month. The Hanafi school requires daily intention. Note the practical difference in validity.
This difference stems from whether the month is seen as a single continuous act of worship or multiple separate acts.

Source Text

(179) No worship is valid without intention. And according to us, intention is necessary for every day, because fasting the month (are separate acts of worship), i.e., the fast of each day is a worship by itself. Do you not see that invalidation in the original does not prevent the validity of the rest? So it is like different prayers, each requires its own intention because between every two days there is something that is not the time of this worship (the nights), so the fast of each day remains a separate worship throughout the month. So it requires multiple intentions according to the number of days, and there is no worship without intention. [Al-Binaayah: Book of Fasting, etc.] Translation: Worship is not realized without intention. Explanation: The meaning of the mentioned principle is that no worship can be realized without intention; rather intention is necessary for worship. The Messenger of Allah ﷺ said: “Actions are by intentions.” The author of al-Hidayah, by mentioning this principle, explains that if a person did not intend fasting during the entire Ramadan and did not break the fast (i.e., he did not intend to fast nor break it, but simply refrained without intention—abstained from eating, drinking, or intercourse), then qadā' of those fasts is obligatory for him. Because in Ramadan, mere abstention is not worship; rather, refraining from the three nullifiers with intention is worship. In the presented case, abstention occurred but intention was not found, so this abstention is not worship. Therefore, his fast is not valid and qadā' is obligatory.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage presents a core principle: intention (niyya) is indispensable for any act to be considered worship. The author cites the famous hadith “Innamā al-aʿmāl bi-n-niyyāt” to support this. The specific application here is fasting: if a person refrains from eating, drinking, and sexual intercourse during Ramadan without intending to fast (e.g., out of habit or without awareness), his abstention (imsāk) is not counted as worship. Therefore, he must make up (qaḍā') those days. The reasoning is that each day's fast is an independent act of worship, separated by nights, so each requires its own intention. The passage also cites al-Binaayah to emphasize that invalidation of one day does not affect others, reinforcing the need for separate intention. The Hanafi position is that intention must be renewed each night for the following day's fast in Ramadan, unlike Imam Malik who held one intention sufficient for the whole month.

Key Terms
نِيَّة (niyya): Intention; a mental resolve to perform an act of worship for Allah.
إمساك (imsāk): Abstention from eating, drinking, and sexual intercourse during fasting hours.
مفطرات ثلاثہ (mufṭirāt thalātha): The three nullifiers of fasting: eating, drinking, and sexual intercourse.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) He is not required to make it up if he was unaware
D) He must pay kaffarah

Key Takeaway
Intention is the condition that transforms physical abstention into the worship of fasting; without it, the fast is invalid and requires qadā'.

Kaffarah is for Breaking the Fast, Not for Refraining
Principle statement with proof from Quran

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between ifsād and imtinā' in fasting and apply the rule that kaffarah is wajib only for deliberately breaking a valid fast.

Core Idea
Kaffarah is due only for destroying an act of worship, not for omitting it.

This lesson establishes a foundational principle in Hanafi fiqh of fasting: the expiation (kaffarah) for breaking a Ramadan fast is tied to actively invalidating an existing fast, not to merely refraining from fasting. Understanding this distinction saves you from incorrectly applying kaffarah to cases of simple neglect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle
The passage states a principle: kaffarah is attached to ifsād, not imtinā'. Understand that ifsād means actively breaking a validly started fast, while imtinā' means not starting or omitting the fast altogether.
2
Locate the proof
The author cites Quran 98:5 to show that worship requires sincerity and intention. Without intention, there is no valid act of worship to break, hence no kaffarah.
Sincerity (ikhlās) necessitates niyyah.
3
Distinguish the consequences
If an act is invalidated after a valid start (ifsād), kaffarah is wajib. If a person never starts the fast (imtinā'), only qadā is required.
Qadā is making up the missed fast.
4
Apply to examples
Test with concrete cases: intentional eating during a valid fast triggers kaffarah; skipping the entire day without intention does not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۱۸۰) إِنَّ الْكَفَّارَةَ تَعَلَّقَتُ بِالْإِفْسَادِ لَا بِالْاِمْتِنَاعِ .
ترجمہ: کفارے کا تعلق روزہ توڑنے سے ہے نہ کہ رکنے سے۔
تشریح: مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ کفارہ کے تعلق روزہ فاسد کرنے سے ہے، امتناع یعنی رکنے کے ساتھ نہیں ہے، یعنی کسی عبادت کو فاسد کرنے کی وجہ سے کفارہ واجب ہوتا ہے،
عبادت سے رکنے کی وجہ سے کفارہ واجب نہیں ہوتا ۔
أَنَّ الْمُسْتَحَقَّ عَلَيْهِ الْإِمْسَاكُ بِجِهَةِ الْعِبَادَةِ لِقَوْلِهِ تَعَالَى وَمَا أَمَرُوا إِلا لِيَعْبُدُوا اللَّهَ مُخْلِصِينَ وَالْإِخْلَاصُ لَا يَكُونُ بِدُونِ النِّيَّةِ.

(180) Verily, kaffarah is attached to breaking (the fast), not to refraining. Translation: Kaffarah is related to breaking the fast, not to refraining. Explanation: The meaning of the mentioned principle is that kaffarah is related to invalidating the fast, not to abstention, i.e., kaffarah becomes obligatory due to invalidating an act of worship, not due to refraining from worship. That what is required is abstention with the intention of worship, because of His statement: 'And they were not commanded except to worship Allah, sincere to Him in religion' (Quran 98:5). And sincerity cannot be without intention. Tabyin al-Haqa'iq, Book of Fasting, Chapter on what nullifies fasting and what does not, Vol. 1, p. 341, Dar al-Kitab al-Islami. Al-Hidayah, Book of Fasting, Chapter on what requires qada' and kaffarah, Section, Vol. 1, p. 242.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage states a principle that kaffarah (expiation) for breaking a fast is only due when the fast is actively broken (ifsād), not when one fails to start or refrains from fasting. For example, if someone deliberately nullifies a valid fast by eating, kaffarah is wajib. But if someone simply does not fast at all (imtināʿ) without having started, no kaffarah is due; only qadā' is required. The principle emphasizes that kaffarah is a penalty for destroying an act of worship, not for omitting it. The proof is Quranic: worship must be done sincerely with intention. If there is no intention, there is no worship to break, so no kaffarah. The passage cites Tabyin al-Haqa'iq and al-Hidayah for this ruling.

Key Terms
كفارة (kaffārah): Expiation; a penalty for deliberately breaking an obligatory fast without valid excuse.
إفساد (ifsād): Invalidating; actively breaking an act of worship after it has validly commenced.
امتناع (imtinā'): Refraining; not performing the act of worship at all.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the principle, why is kaffarah not obligatory on a person who simply does not fast during Ramadan?
A) Because kaffarah is only for breaking a fast that was validly started
B) Because he did not eat or drink
C) Because he made intention to fast
D) Because the Quran does not mention kaffarah

Key Takeaway
The principle: Kaffarah is wajib only for deliberately invalidating an existing valid fast, not for omitting a fast altogether.

Worship Requires Intention (Niyyah)
States and proves the principle that worship requires intention.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that intention is a condition for the validity of fasting, and apply it to the case of a person who refrained without intention.

Core Idea
Intention distinguishes worship from habit; without it, even complete abstention is not a valid fast.

This lesson examines a fundamental axiom: acts of worship require intention (niyyah). Drawing on the famous hadith 'Actions are but by intentions' and a concrete scenario from fasting, we see why mere physical abstention does not constitute a valid fast. The Hanafi school emphasizes that intention is the decisive factor that transforms an ordinary act into an act of devotion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its application
Recognize that the passage states a general principle: worship requires intention, then applies it to fasting.
The hadith 'Actions are but by intentions' is the proof.
2
Analyze the scenario
Consider the case of a person who refrains in Ramadan without intention. Understand that mere abstention (imsak) is not sufficient without intention.
The passage distinguishes between aspects of worship and habit.
3
Trace the proof structure
Note that the hadith establishes the rule, and the verse about sincerity reinforces it.
The phrase 'innama' indicates restriction.
4
Derive the consequence
Since intention is missing, the fast is invalid, and qada becomes obligatory.
This is a direct application of the principle.

ترجمہ: بغیر نیت کے عبادت متحقق نہیں ہوتی۔
تشریح مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ بغیر نیت کے کوئی عبادت متحقق نہیں ہوسکتی، بلکہ عبادت کے لئے نیت ضروری ہے ، رسول اللہ صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم نے ارشاد فرمایا: إِنَّمَا الْأَعْمَالُ بِالنِّيَّاتِ“ صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلا ر ہے ہیں کہ اگر کسی نے پورے رمضان میں روزوں کی نیت نہیں کی اور نہ افطار کی ، یعنی نہ روزے کی نیت کی اور نہ افطار کی بلکہ ویسے بغیر نیت کے امساک اختیار کیا یعنی کھانے پینے یا جماع سے رکا رہا، تو اس شخص پر روزوں کی قضا واجب ہے، اس لئے کہ رمضان میں مطلق امساک عبادت نہیں ہے بلکہ نیت کے ساتھ مفطرات ثلاثہ سے رکنا عبادت ہے۔ صورتِ مسئلہ میں امساک تو پایا گیا مگر نیت نہیں پائی گئی ، اس لئے مذکورہ امساک عبادت نہیں ہو گا۔ اس لئے کہ روزے میں مفطرات ثلاثہ سے جو امساک متعلق ہے وہ عبادت کی جہت سے ہو نہ کہ عادت کی جہت سے اور امساک من جبہتہ العبادۃ اور من جہتہ العادۃ میں فرق نیت سے ہوتا ہے اور بغیر نیت کے عبادت نہیں ہوتی لہذا جب اس نے نیت نہیں کی تو اس کا روزہ بھی نہ ہوگا اور اس پر قضاء واجب ہے۔
أَنَّ الْمُسْتَحَقَّ عَلَيْهِ الْإِمْسَاكُ بِجِهَةِ الْعِبَادَةِ لِقَوْلِهِ تَعَالَى وَمَا أُمِرُوا إِلا لِيَعْبُدُوا اللَّهَ مُخْلِصِينَ وَالْإِخْلَاصُ لَا يَكُونُ بِدُونِ النِّيَّةِ.
تبيين الحقائق كتاب الصوم، باب ما يفسد الصوم وما لا يفسده، ج ۱ ص ۳۴۱، ط: دار الكتاب الإسلامي]
الهداية: كتاب الصوم، باب ما يوجب القضاء والكفارة ، فصل، ج ۱ ص ۲۴۲، ط: رحمانيه
Translation: Without intention, worship is not realized. Explanation: The meaning of the aforementioned principle is that no worship can be realized without intention; rather, intention is necessary for worship. The Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, said: 'Actions are but by intentions.' The author of Al-Hidayah, mentioning the aforementioned principle, is explaining that if a person did not intend the fasts of the entire Ramadan and did not break the fast, i.e., he did not intend the fast and did not break it, but merely refrained without intention, i.e., he stopped eating, drinking, or having intercourse, then qada (making up) of the fasts is obligatory upon him, because absolute refraining in Ramadan is not worship; rather, refraining from the three nullifiers with intention is worship. In this case, refraining was found but intention was not found, so this refraining is not worship because the refraining from the three nullifiers in fasting is from the aspect of worship, not from the aspect of habit, and the difference between refraining from the aspect of worship and from the aspect of habit is made by intention, and without intention there is no worship. Therefore, when he did not intend, his fast is not valid and qada is obligatory upon him.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof. It states the fundamental principle that intention (niyyah) is essential for the validity of any act of worship. The commentator quotes the famous hadith, 'Actions are but by intentions,' as the primary proof. He then applies this principle to a specific case: a person who refrains from eating, drinking, and sexual intercourse during the entire month of Ramadan but never makes an intention to fast. Despite the physical abstention, this does not count as a valid fast because the act of refraining must be directed as an act of worship, which requires intention. The distinction between refraining as an act of worship (min jihat al-ibada) and refraining as a mere habit (min jihat al-ada) is intention. Therefore, such a person must make up (qada) the missed fasts. The passage also includes a supporting Qur'anic verse (Quran 98:5) about sincerity, which further underscores the necessity of intention.

Key Terms
نیت (niyyah): Intention, the mental resolve to perform a specific act of worship for the sake of Allah.
امساک (imsak): Refraining from eating, drinking, and sexual intercourse.
قضاء (qada): Making up a missed obligatory fast after its prescribed time.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example given in the passage, why is qada (making up) wajib for the person who refrained from eating and drinking all day in Ramadan without intention?
A) Because he broke the fast by eating something.
B) Because his refraining was not accompanied by intention, so it was not a valid fast.
C) Because he did not fast for the entire month.
D) Because he ate intentionally after thinking his fast was broken.

Key Takeaway
Intention is the essential element that transforms abstention into worship; without it, no valid fast occurs and qada is required.

Kaffara Requires a Valid Fast to Be Broken
States the principle that kaffara requires a valid fast to break

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that kaffara is only due when a valid fast is deliberately broken and apply it to distinguish between qada and kaffara.

Core Idea
Kaffara is for breaking a valid fast, not for failing to fast.

This passage from Al-Hidayah clarifies a common confusion in the laws of Ramadan fasting. Many assume that any eating during Ramadan without excuse incurs both qada and kaffara, but the Hanafi school rules that kaffara only applies when a validly initiated fast is intentionally broken. The difference hinges on intention: without it, there is no valid fast to break.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Principle
Locate the key phrase: 'إن الكفارة تعلقت بالإفساد لا بالامتناع' – expiation is tied to spoiling (ifsad), not to refraining (imtina').
This is the foundational rule of the passage.
2
Understand the Scenario
The author gives an example: a person did not intend to fast at night, entered the morning without intention, then ate or drank. Determine the ruling according to the principle.
The person never had a valid fast because intention is a prerequisite.
3
Apply the Principle
Since a valid fast never began, the act of eating does not constitute 'ifsad' (breaking). Therefore, kaffara is not wajib. Only qada is required for not fasting at all.
Qada is owed for missing the fast, not for breaking it.
4
Recognize the Consequence
The passage concludes that kaffara is based on breaking a valid act of worship, not on simply refraining.
This distinguishes the ruling from other schools that may consider mere abstention sinful.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۱۸۰) إِنَّ الْكَفَّارَةَ تَعَلَّقَتُ بِالْإِفْسَادِ لَا بِالْاِمْتِنَاعِ .
ترجمہ: کفارے کا تعلق روزہ توڑنے سے ہے نہ کہ رکنے سے۔
تشریح: مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ کفارہ کے تعلق روزہ فاسد کرنے سے ہے، امتناع یعنی رکنے کے ساتھ نہیں ہے، یعنی کسی عبادت کو فاسد کرنے کی وجہ سے کفارہ واجب ہوتا ہے، عبادت سے رکنے کی وجہ سے کفارہ واجب نہیں ہوتا ۔ صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلا رہے ہیں کہ اگر کسی نے رمضان میں روزے کی نیت نہیں کی اور اسی حال میں صبح کی ، پھر صبح کو کچھ کھا پی لیا تو اس شخص پر روزے کی قضاء ہوگی کفارہ نہیں ہوگا۔ قضاء اس لئے ہوگی کہ اس نے روزہ نہیں رکھا اور روزہ نہ رکھنے سے قضاء واجب ہوتی ہے۔ کفارہ اس لئے واجب نہیں ہے کہ کفارہ کا سبب روزہ توڑنا ہے اور صورتِ مسئلہ میں جب اس شخص نے روزے کی نیت ہی نہیں کی تھی تو اس کا روزہ ہی نہیں ہوا، کیونکہ بغیر نیت کے روزہ متحقق نہیں ہوتا ، جب یہ روزہ ہی نہیں ہے تو کھانے پینے سے بھلا یہ کیسے ٹوٹے گا؟ اس لئے صورت میں روزہ توڑنا نہیں پایا گیا، اور ضابطہ یہ ہے کہ روزہ توڑنے سے کفارہ آتا ہے نہ کہ رکھنے سے رکنے پر۔ أن الكفارة تعلقت بالإفساد أى بإفساد الصوم وهذا امتناع أى عن الصوم لا إفساد له (إدَ لا صوم إلا بالنية فلا كفارة عليه لأنه غير صائم.
[البناية: كتاب الصوم، باب ما يوجب القضاء والكفارة، فصل، ج ۴، ص ۹۹، ط: دار الكتب العلمية]
الهداية: كتاب الصوم، باب ما يوجب القضاء والكفارة، فصل، ج ۱ ص ۲۴۴، ط: رحمانیہ
(180) Verily, expiation (kaffara) is connected to causing corruption (ifsad), not to refraining (imtina'). Translation: Expiation is related to breaking the fast, not to refraining. Explanation: The meaning of the aforementioned principle is that expiation is related to spoiling the fast, not to refraining, i.e., expiation becomes obligatory due to spoiling an act of worship, not due to refraining from worship. The author of Al-Hidayah, mentioning the aforementioned principle, is explaining that if a person did not intend the fast in Ramadan and entered the morning in that state, then ate or drank something in the morning, then upon him is qada of the fast, not kaffara. Qada is because he did not fast, and not fasting makes qada wajib. Kaffara is not wajib because the cause of kaffara is breaking the fast, and in this case, since the person did not intend the fast, his fast did not occur at all. Because without intention, the fast is not realized. When the fast itself does not exist, how can eating and drinking break it? Therefore, breaking of the fast is not found, and the rule is that kaffara comes from breaking the fast, not from refraining. The expiation is connected to breaking, i.e., spoiling the fast, and this is refraining from the fast, not spoiling it. Since there is no fast without intention, there is no kaffara upon him because he is not a faster.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling with an illustrative example. The key principle is that expiation (kaffara) for fasting is triggered by intentionally breaking a valid fast, not by merely failing to fast. The commentator explains that if a person does not make an intention to fast at night and then eats something the next morning, he must make up the day (qada) but does not owe expiation. The reason is that a valid fast never started due to lack of intention, so there was nothing to break. The rule is explicitly stated: kaffara is for ifsad (spoiling a valid worship), not for imtina' (simple refraining). The passage also quotes from al-Binayah to reinforce that without intention, there is no fast and thus no kaffara.

Key Terms
كفارة (kaffarah): Expiation; a specific prescribed act of worship (e.g., freeing a slave, fasting two consecutive months, or feeding the poor) required upon intentionally breaking a Ramadan fast without valid excuse.
إفساد (ifsad): Spoiling or causing corruption; in fasting, it refers to breaking a valid fast deliberately.
امتناع (imtina'): Refraining or abstaining; in this context, simply not fasting without having intended to fast.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the person who did not intend to fast at night and then eats in the morning not owe kaffara?
A) Because he ate unintentionally.
B) Because his fast was not valid from the beginning, so there was no valid fast to break.
C) Because kaffara is only for not fasting entirely.
D) Because he made up for it by fasting later.

Key Takeaway
Kaffara in fasting is only for breaking a valid fast; without intention, there is no fast to break.

Unsupported Conjecture Has No Legal Weight: The Case of Cupping and Fasting
States a legal maxim and its application to fasting.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principle that unsupported conjecture (zann) is not legally considered in determining whether a fast is broken, and explain why both qada and kaffara are required for a person who intentionally eats based on a mistaken belief.

Core Idea
Conjecture without legal proof is worthless; a mistaken belief does not exempt one from kaffara.

This passage from Al-Hidayah establishes a foundational principle: mere personal opinion or assumption (zann) that is not grounded in a recognized legal proof (dalil shar'i) carries no weight in Islamic law. The author applies this to a fasting scenario: a person undergoes cupping, wrongly believes it breaks the fast, and then intentionally eats. Understanding this rule helps us see why both making up the fast (qada) and expiation (kaffara) become obligatory, and why sincere ignorance is not an excuse when the belief lacks shar'i basis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Principle
The passage begins with a rule: conjecture without legal proof is not considered (mu'tabar). Understand that this is a general usul principle applied in fiqh.
2
Apply to Cupping Case
The example: a person gets cupped (hijama) and mistakenly thinks it invalidates the fast. Because this belief has no shar'i basis, it is ignored.
3
Determine the Ruling
Since the person then intentionally eats, the fast is considered deliberately broken. Therefore, both qada (make-up) and kaffara (expiation) are wajib.
4
Understand the Rationale
Cupping does not break the fast because breaking involves entry of something into the stomach, not exit. The person's belief is baseless.
5
Note the Condition
The ruling presupposes a valid fast (with intention). If no intention was made, no fast existed, thus no kaffara.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۱۸۱) إِنَّ الظَّنَّ إِذَا لَمْ يَسْتَنِدُ إِلَى دَلِيْلٍ شَرْعِي فَهُوَ لَيْسَ بِمُعْتَبَرٍ . ترجمہ: جو گمان دلیل شرعی کی طرف منسوب نہ ہو وہ معتبر نہیں ہے۔
تشریح: مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ جب گمان دلیل شرعی سے ثابت نہ ہو تو اس کا اعتبار نہیں ہوگا۔ صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول ذکر کر کے یہ بتلا رہے ہیں کہ اگر کسی نے پچھنے لگوائے اور یہ گمان کیا کہ پچھنے لگانے سے روزہ ٹوٹ جاتا ہے پھر قصداً کچھ کھایا تو اس پر قضاء اور کفارہ دونوں واجب ہوں گے۔ اس لئے کہ اس آدمی کا یہ گمان کہ پچھنے سے روزہ ٹوٹ جاتا ہے یہ کسی دلیل شرعی کی طرف منسوب نہیں ہے اور جو گمان دلیل شرعی کی طرف منسوب نہ ہو وہ معتبر نہیں ہوتا ،لہذا یہ گمان بھی معتبر نہ ہو گا۔ روزہ ٹوٹنے کا تعلق کسی چیز کے پیٹ میں داخل ہونے سے ہے نہ کہ خارج ہونے سے، پچھنے لگوانے سے تو فاسد خون خارج ہوتا ہے، اس لئے یہ مفسد صوم نہیں ہے، جبکہ مذکورہ مسئلہ میں اس نے مفسد سمجھ کر کھا پی لیا تو یہ عمد رمضان میں کھانے پینے کی طرح ہو گیا ، اور عمداً کھانے أن الكفارة تعلقت بالإفساد أى بإفساد الصوم وهذا امتناع أى عن الصوم لا إفساد له (إدَ لا صوم إلا بالنية فلا كفارة عليه لأنه غير صائم.
[البناية: كتاب الصوم، باب ما يوجب القضاء والكفارة، فصل، ج ۴، ص ۹۹، ط: دار الكتب العلمية]
الهداية: كتاب الصوم، باب ما يوجب القضاء والكفارة، فصل، ج ۱ ص ۲۴۴، ط: رحمانیہ
(181) Verily, conjecture (zann) that is not based on a legal proof (dalil shar'i) is not considered (mu'tabar). Translation: A supposition that is not attributed to a legal proof is not considered. Explanation: The meaning of the aforementioned principle is that when a conjecture is not proven by a legal proof, it will not be given consideration. The author of Al-Hidayah, mentioning the aforementioned principle, is explaining that if a person underwent cupping and thought that cupping breaks the fast, then intentionally ate something, then both qada and kaffara are wajib upon him. Because this person's conjecture that cupping breaks the fast is not attributed to any legal proof, and a conjecture not attributed to a legal proof is not considered; therefore, this conjecture is not considered. The breaking of the fast is related to something entering the stomach, not to something exiting. Cupping only removes impure blood, so it is not a nullifier of the fast. In this case, since he ate and drank thinking it to be a nullifier, it becomes like intentional eating and drinking in Ramadan. And intentionally eating: expiation is connected to breaking the fast, i.e., spoiling the fast, and this is refraining from the fast, not spoiling it. Since there is no fast without intention, there is no kaffara upon him because he is not a faster.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling and its application. The principle is that mere personal opinion (zann) that is not supported by a textual or rational proof from Shariah carries no legal weight. The commentator applies this to a case: a person undergoes cupping (hijama) and mistakenly believes that cupping invalidates the fast. Based on this unfounded belief, he then intentionally eats, assuming his fast is already broken. The ruling is that both qada and kaffara are obligatory. The reason is that cupping does not actually break the fast according to the Hanafi school, since breaking the fast involves entry (of food/drink) into the stomach, not exit of blood. The person's mistaken belief is not based on a legal proof, so it is disregarded. Since he intentionally ate in Ramadan with the intention of breaking the fast, it is considered deliberate breaking of a valid fast (if he had validly started the fast). However, the passage also reiterates that kaffara is only due if a valid fast was broken; here, the person must have had intention to fast for the fast to be valid initially. The commentary includes a quote from al-Binayah stressing that without intention, there is no fast and thus no kaffara. So the ruling assumes the person had made intention for fasting that day.

Key Terms
ظن (zann): Conjecture, personal opinion, or supposition that is not firmly grounded in evidence.
دليل شرعي (dalil shar'i): A legal proof from the Qur'an, Sunnah, Ijma, or Qiyas that is accepted as a source of Shariah rulings.
معتبر (mu'tabar): Considered, given legal weight, accepted as valid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school require both qada and kaffara for the person who underwent cupping and then ate?
A) Because cupping breaks the fast.
B) Because his belief about cupping is legally unsupported, so the fast was valid, and intentional eating breaks it.
C) Because he did not intend to fast.
D) Because he ate unintentionally.

Key Takeaway
Unsupported personal opinion (zann) has no legal weight; only beliefs backed by shar'i proof can affect the rulings of fasting.

The Principle of Zann Without Shar‘i Evidence is Invalid
Explaining a legal principle and its application to cupping and fasting.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the principle that speculation without shar‘i evidence is legally invalid and apply it to determine the ruling when someone mistakenly believes an act breaks the fast.

Core Idea
A speculation based on no evidence has no legal effect—only evidence-based reasoning matters in fiqh.

This lesson introduces a fundamental legal principle: mere speculation (zann) that is not grounded in shar‘i evidence carries no weight in Islamic law. The author of al-Hidayah uses this principle to address a specific case: a person who undergoes cupping, mistakenly believes it breaks the fast, and then intentionally eats. Understanding this principle helps you distinguish between valid excuses and unfounded assumptions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle
The passage begins with a general rule: speculation not based on shar‘i evidence is invalid. State this rule in your own words.
2
Apply the principle to the case
The person speculated that cupping breaks the fast, but this speculation has no shar‘i basis. How does the principle apply?
The principle makes his speculation irrelevant.
3
Determine the ruling
Since his speculation is invalid, his intentional eating is considered a deliberate violation, requiring qada and kaffarah.
Both are wajib (obligatory).
4
Identify the evidence
What proof supports the Hanafi position that cupping does not break the fast? The hadith in Sahih al-Bukhari: the Prophet (peace be upon him) cupped while fasting.
Dalil: action of the Prophet.
5
Note the contrast
How would other schools rule? Imam al-Awza‘i holds that cupping breaks the fast, so his ruling would differ.
Cross-madhhab difference.
